of this section 6409 approval, and (2) other Claims of any kind or form, whether for

mounts, brackets, hardware, cables, wires, conduits, structures, shelters, towers, poles,

affected by the removal work to a standard compliant with applicable laws. In establishing

California Government Code § 65964(a), the City shall take into consideration any 
information provided by the permittee regarding the cost to remove the wireless facility to 
a standard compliant with applicable laws. The performance bond shall expressly survive 
the duration of the permit term to the extent required to effectuate a complete removal of 
the subject wireless facility in accordance with this condition.

continues after notice and reasonable opportunity to cure the noncompliance, the City 
may revoke this section 6409 approval or amend these conditions as the City deems 
necessary or appropriate to correct any such noncompliance.

complete and accurate copies of all permits and other regulatory approvals issued in 
connection with the wireless facility, which includes without limitation this approval, the 
approved plans and photo simulations incorporated into this approval, all conditions 
associated with this approval and any ministerial permits or approvals issued in

copies or electronic records kept in the City’s regular files will control over any conflicts 
between such City-controlled copies or records and the permittee’s electronic copies, and 
complete originals will control over all other copies in any form.

Undergrounded Utilities. In the event that other public utilities or cable television

antennas and antenna supports, to the extent technically feasible. Such undergrounding 
shall occur at the permittee’s sole cost and expense except as reimbursed pursuant to 
law. In areas with existing overhead lines, new communication lines shall be “overlashed” 
with existing communication lines to the extent technically feasible.

or off any water, oil, gas, electricity or other utility service in connection with the

species. Only ISA Certified workers under the supervision of a licensed arborist shall be 
used to install the replacement tree(s). The box size and other standards for any 
replacement trees shall be subject to the City’s approval in consultation with the licensed 
arborist. The permittee shall, at all times, be responsible to maintain any replacement 
landscape features.

AT&T’s Comments on San Mateo, CA’s Wireless Communications Facilities in the Public 
Right-of-Way Ordinance and Design Standards  
 
• AT&T thanks the city for considering changes to its wireless regulations. AT&T offers 
the following comments and summary of key legal concepts to help the city revise its 
regulations to foster responsible deployments and to fully comply with applicable laws. 
 
Key Legal Concepts 
 
• The Federal Telecommunications Act of 1996 (“Act”) establishes key limitations on local 
regulations. Under the Act, the city must not prohibit or effectively prohibit wireless 
services. See 47 U.S.C. §§ 253(a), 332(c)(7)(B)(i)(II). The city must act “within a 
reasonable period of time” on each application. 47 U.S.C. § 332(c)(7)(B)(ii).  
 
• The FCC has codified “shot clocks” – presumptive maximum timeframes for processing 
applications. Violations of the shot clocks may result in unlawful effective prohibitions 
under the Act, and can result in deemed approval under state law. Cal. Govt. Code § 
65964.1(a). Similarly, eligible facilities requests (EFRs) are deemed granted under 
federal law when the city fails to approve within the shot clock. The FCC has made clear 
that the city must grant all necessary approvals and authorizations within the applicable 
shot clock,1 and the shot clock applies “to all aspects of and steps in the siting process.”2 
 
• Section 6409(a) of the 2012 Spectrum Act, 47 U.S.C. § 1455(a), requires the city to 
approve modifications to the extent they do not substantially change the physical 
dimensions of the existing facilities. The FCC has established objective criteria to 
determine whether a proposal is an EFR that must be approved (see 47 C.F.R. § 
1.6100(b)(7)), and the FCC has established procedures, including a 60-day shot clock and 
a deemed grant remedy for failure to timely approve an EFR (see 47 C.F.R. § 1.6100(c)). 
The FCC also released two orders in 2020 that provide additional interpretation and 
guidance on EFRs that the city must approve. 
 
• The FCC’s Small Cell Order established an aesthetic standard for small cells. To be 
lawful, aesthetic requirements must be reasonable, i.e., technically feasible and published 
in advance.3 
 
• The FCC’s Small Cell Order also established a standard and safe harbor for lawful fees 
for small cell applications. To be lawful and passed on to applicants, fees must be 
reasonable, cost-based, and nondiscriminatory. Excessive fees cannot be charged to 
applicants even if they represent true costs.4 The FCC’s safe harbor sets the ceiling for 
 
1 See Accelerating Wireless Broadband Deployment by Removing Barriers to Infrastructure Investment, Declaratory 
Ruling and Third Report and Order, FCC 18-133, 30 FCC Rcd 9088 at ¶¶ 132-137. (September 27, 2018) (“Small 
Cell Order”). 
2 Id. at ¶ 132. 
3 Id. at ¶ 50. 
4 Id. at ¶ 70. Federal courts have reached the same conclusion with respect to applications for other wireless facilities 
as well. 
340 of 445

--- page 341 ---
City of San Mateo 
June 7, 2021 
Page 2 of 8 
 
presumptively reasonable fees, and the city bears the burden to show that any additional 
fees are justified under the fee standard. The Ninth Circuit U.S. Court of Appeals has 
upheld the FCC’s fee standard, including the FCC’s safe harbor fee amounts. 
 
• AT&T has a statewide franchise right to access and construct telecommunications 
facilities in the public rights-of-way under Public Utilities Code Section 7901. 
AT&T’s Comments 
 
• Placements of Freestanding Poles. Banning placement of facilities on freestanding poles 
in residential areas, “near” schools, and parks is too imprecise and creates a substantial 
risk of effectively prohibiting wireless services. Considering the layout of San Mateo, this 
requirement will likely result in broad prohibitions of service in violation of the 
Telecommunications Act of 1996. There are large residential parts of the city where well-
designed small cells will need to be sited to meet customer needs, so the city should 
amend its design standards to express siting preferences rather than prohibitions. Further, 
to the extent these restrictions amount to an indirect means to address fears about radio 
frequency emissions, they are preempted by federal law. Moreover, a 250-foot separation 
between sites on freestanding poles is unreasonable in areas with high network traffic 
(such as intersections).  
 
AT&T’s statement is not accurate. The City standards do not ban placement of facilities 
on freestanding poles in residential areas, near schools, or parks. Provision #13 of the 
Placement Requirements provides that applicants shall “avoid, when reasonably feasible, 
placement in residential areas, near schools, and parks.” There is nothing in the 
applicable federal law or FCC Orders which prevents the City from having such a 
preference for pole placement.  The City does not believe that such a preference would 
effectively prohibit wireless services.  
 
• Prohibited Attachments. The design standards improperly ban attachments to non-
standard, decorative streetlights, wooden streetlights, or traffic signals. The city must 
avoid restrictions that could broadly prohibit service in certain parts of the city. Traffic 
light attachments preserve aesthetics by allowing providers to cover busy areas in 
multiple directions from one site. And the city should allow new or replacement 
decorative poles housing wireless facilities that are designed to look similar to nearby 
decorative poles. 
 
The FCC has confirmed that cities may impose certain aesthetic considerations as long as 
they are reasonable, no more burdensome than those applied to other types of 
infrastructure deployments, and objective. 
 
The City’s design standards, including the limitations on where facilities may be located,  
are intended to protect and promote the health, safety and aesthetic/visual character of the 
City and its neighborhoods. The City believes that these design standards adequately 
comply with the FCC’s limitations. Additionally, the City does not believe that these 
restrictions would in any way “effectively prohibit” a carrier’s ability to provide service 
341 of 445

--- page 342 ---
City of San Mateo 
June 7, 2021 
Page 3 of 8 
 
given the limited nature of the location restrictions, as well as the numerous other 
locations where wireless carriers may install their facilities.  
 
• Fees. The city needs to bring its fees for wireless permits in line with federal law. The 
fees far exceed the FCC’s safe harbor for lawful small cell fees, and the city has not 
provided justification for the fees. The city also should not incur $4,000 in EFR review 
costs. Federal law mandates approval of EFRs, and the FCC has provided applicable 
objective criteria for review of EFRs, so only minimal time and resources would be 
needed to review EFR requests. 
 
The $4,000 is a deposit only, and not an actual charge.  
 
Additionally, the FCC specifically provides that fees higher than the safe harbor levels 
“would be permissible under Sections 253 and 332 to the extent a locality’s actual, 
reasonable costs” are also higher. (FCC 2018 Order, paragraph 32). 
 
The City’s fees comply with the FCC ruling: the fees are a reasonable approximation of 
the City’s costs; only objectively reasonable costs will be assessed; and fees are no higher 
than fees charged to similarly-situated competitors in similar situations.  
 
• Eligible Facilities Requests. Many of the city’s application requirements apply to EFRs. 
Federal law, however, expressly limits the scope of the city’s review of EFRs, so the city 
can only require information reasonably related to determining whether a request 
qualifies as an EFR. The city’s application requirements and checklist seek more 
documents and information than is necessary to determine whether a request is for an 
EFR. The city must limit its requirements to the Section 6409 Evaluation form outlined in 
its application checklist. 
 
The City would like to note that it already does not require certain items for Section 6409 
requests. These items are indicated on the checklist, using an asterix(*), and include the 
Project Purpose (Att 10) and Alternative Sites Analysis (Att 11). 
 
As for the other required items, the City believes that they are within its authority. 
Section 6409 requires local agencies to approve collocation on existing facilities and the 
removal or replacement of existing facilities where there is not a substantial change to the 
physical dimensions of the facilities. However, all EFRs must still comply with all 
generally applicable laws; many of the items on the checklist are necessary to 
demonstrate compliance with such laws. Additionally, a city may require information to 
show that Section 6409(a) applies (including building and safety code compliance).  
Moreover, although a City’s review may be limited for EFRs, this does not prohibit a city 
from still imposing certain conditions of approval. 
 
 
 
342 of 445

--- page 343 ---
City of San Mateo 
June 7, 2021 
Page 4 of 8 
 
• Duration of an EFR Permit. The city cannot limit an EFR permit’s duration to the term of 
the underlying permit because California law requires a minimum term of 10 years for 
wireless siting approvals.  
 
The permit says it’s valid for 10 years.   
 
• Project Purpose and Technical Objective (Non-EFRs). The city requires applicants to 
describe technical objectives, submit propagation maps, and provide a written narrative 
describing the uses of its facilities as part of an application package. Providers should not 
need to justify or demonstrate the need of a proposed facility. Indeed, the FCC has 
rejected all “coverage gap” tests, including specifically rejecting the judicially-created 
least intrusive means test for filling a coverage gap.5 Moreover, the required information 
doesn’t make sense for small cell deployments.  
 
The City requires this information to consider its preferences and the availability of least 
intrusive means to mitigate a significant gap in the provider’s service as part of its 
aesthetic concerns. The FCC’s Small Cell Order also acknowledges local aesthetic 
concerns in fn. 94 and within the Order. 
 
Additionally, there is nothing in the FCC small cell order that preempts the City from still 
requiring information regarding the proposed project’s technical service objectives. 
Although the FCC may disfavor “decisions that have applied solely a ‘coverage gap’- 
based approach under Section 332(c)(7)(B)(i)(II)” (see FCC Order ¶ 40), this does not 
mean that the FCC intended to completely limit a city’s consideration of or a city’s 
access to such information. Many other cities also require such similar information. 
 
That being said, and to respond to AT&T’s comments, the City has edited the Design 
Standards to remove the requirements in Appendix B regarding propagation maps. 
 
• Alternative Site Analysis (Small Cells). Rather than requiring an alternative site analysis 
for each wireless permit application, AT&T echoes Verizon’s suggestion that the city 
narrow its requirement to evaluating any more preferred locations within 250 feet of the 
provider’s chosen site, and to demonstrate that those locations are technically infeasible 
or unavailable.  
 
The City is not aware of any law which would restrict it from imposing a 500 foot search 
ring radius. This radius is required by other neighboring cities in the area as well.  
 
• Accessory Equipment Volume (Small Cells). The city must allow up to 28 cubic feet of 
accessory equipment to be consistent with 47 C.F.R. § 1.6002(l)(3). 
 
 
5 Small Cell Order at ¶ 40, n.94. 
343 of 445

--- page 344 ---
City of San Mateo 
June 7, 2021 
Page 5 of 8 
 
47 CFR 1.6002(l)(3) provides that all wireless equipment associated with a small cell 
wireless facility structure may not exceed 28 cubic feet in volume. However, the statute 
does not prohibit a City from limiting the allowable volume further.   
 
• Neighborhood Meeting. Strongly encouraging a neighborhood meeting is unnecessary, 
likely unproductive, and serves only to drive up costs and risk shot clock compliance. 
This is especially so for EFRs, which the city must promptly approve. 
 
The FCC Order specifically states that the FCC “encourage[s] voluntary pre-application 
discussions”. (See FCC Order ¶ 146). Additionally, the FCC stated that such meetings 
“can clarify key aspects of the application review process … and may speed the pace of 
review.” Moreover, the FCC clarified that such voluntary pre-application discussions 
would not start the shot clock; (“…the shot clock will begin when an application is filed, 
presumably after the pre-application review has concluded.”( ¶ 146)). 
 
The neighborhood meeting discussed in the City’s regulations is voluntary, and therefore 
would not trigger the shot clock. The City believes that the neighborhood meetings would 
be very productive and helpful for applicants to understand the concerns and preferences 
of the community.  
 
• Consultants. The city needs to be careful and limit its use of consultants to technical 
matters, such as structural analysis. The FCC has cautioned municipalities against 
excessive charges, including fees for consultant review, which cannot be passed on to 
applicants. 
 
The FCC does not state that consultant fees may not be recovered from the applicant. The 
FCC only cautions cities from charging “fees that recover more than the state or local 
costs associated with facilities deployment—or that are based on unreasonable costs, 
such as exorbitant consultant fees or the like—”. (FCC Order ¶56). The City’s fees are 
not based on any exorbitant consultant fees.  Cities are permitted to recover “a reasonable 
approximation of their costs related to the deployment of Small Wireless Facilities.” 
(FCC Order ¶56).   
 
• Notice. A 500-foot radius for public noticing and a requirement to maintain a log of all 
communications from residents is excessive for low-power, low-profile small cells. A 
notice requirement for EFRs is also unnecessary because the city “may not deny, and 
shall approve” applications for EFRs that meet the requirements of Section 6409(a). 
 
The City’s 500-foot radius for public noticing matches neighboring cities’ notification 
requirements for wireless facilities. Additionally, interested parties should still be 
provided notice of EFRs, regardless of the approval process.   
 
• Appeals. The city’s appeal process sets the city up to violate the shot clock on a regular 
basis resulting in effective prohibition for small cells and deemed grants for EFRs. 
 
344 of 445

--- page 345 ---
City of San Mateo 
June 7, 2021 
Page 6 of 8 
 
The City took into consideration the FCC shot clocks when drafting the appeal process, 
and fully intends to meet such deadlines. It is for this reason that interested parties were 
limited to only 5 calendar days to file an appeal.  
 
• Less Obtrusive Designs. AT&T asks that the city clarify that it will not require providers 
to upgrade small cells using “less obtrusive” designs in the middle of a permit term 
(which cannot be shorter than ten years per state law). AT&T also cautions the city 
against dictating technology, which is preempted by federal law. 
 
The City’s “Least Obtrusive” aesthetic condition is with regards to a wireless facility’s 
“appearance, size, and location”. The City has the authority to impose aesthetic 
conditions as long as they are reasonable. The City is not dictating technology as AT&T 
is suggesting.  
 
The City is not sure which provision AT&T’s comment is in reference to exactly. Except 
for perhaps a public safety issue, the City does not believe the guidelines require a 
provider to change their design in the middle of a permit term.   
 
 
• Pole Top Antennas. The city should reconsider requiring antennas to be placed on top of 
poles, which will unlawfully prohibit wireless service by unduly limiting technology and 
equipment configurations. And side-mounted antennas are sometimes required due to 
space constraints on existing utility poles. 
 
The City made the following edits (new language indicated with underline): 
 
13. Antenna Locations.  To the maximum extent technically feasible, antennas shall be 
placed at the top of the pole and shall be shrouded to the maximum extent feasible. 
 
The City also added the following provisions regarding antennas:  
 
14. Shrouding. All antennas and associated cables, jumpers, wires, mounts, masts, 
brackets and other connectors and hardware shall be installed within a single shroud or 
radome to the maximum extent technically feasible. For wireless facilities on utility 
poles, all antennas shall be shrouded, and located at the top of the pole or strand-
mounted, to the maximum extent technically feasible. For pole-top antennas, the shroud 
shall not exceed 2.5 times the median pole diameter and must taper down to pole. For 
side-arm antennas, the shroud shall cover the cross arm and any cables, jumpers, wires or 
other connectors between the vertical riser and the antenna. 
 
15. Antenna Volume. Each individual antenna associated with a single wireless facility 
shall not exceed 3 cubic feet. The cumulative volume for all antennas on a single wireless 
facility shall not exceed: (A) 3 cubic feet in residential areas; or (B) 6 cubic feet in 
nonresidential areas.

--- page 346 ---
City of San Mateo 
June 7, 2021 
Page 7 of 8 
 
16. Horizontal Projection. Side-mounted antennas, where permitted, shall not project: (A) 
more than 2 feet from the support structure; (B) over any roadway for vehicular travel; or 
(C) over any abutting private property. If applicable laws require a side-mounted antenna 
to project more than 2 feet from the support structure, the projection shall be no greater 
than required for compliance with such laws. Pole-mounted antennas and accessory 
equipment should be installed in a manner that minimizes horizontal projections, which 
includes, without limitation, installing equipment within the pole or as flush to the pole as 
possible. 
 
• Network Map. The city should not require an applicant’s network map identifying future 
facilities planned within two years. AT&T does not have a list of facilities planned far 
into the future as its facility plans are constantly developing to address network needs. 
 
The City is only requesting “future wireless facilities that are reasonably foreseeable 
within two years of the application submission.”  
 
• Locating Hardware Inside the Pole. The city requires conduit, wires, and other hardware 
to be located within the pole. To avoid prohibitions, these types of requirements should 
be revised by adding “to the extent feasible.” 
 
The City has the authority to impose aesthetic conditions as long as they are reasonable. 
The FCC specifies that aesthetic requirements may be imposed to help a city avoid “the 
intangible public harm of unsightly or out-of-character” wireless facilities. 
 
• Undergrounding. Requirements that equipment be installed underground need to be 
limited to the extent reasonably feasible and practical. Antennas and radios need to be 
above ground near enough to one another to function properly. And undergrounding 
requirements are unnecessary for stealth sites. 
 
The City may require undergrounding requirements as long as such requirements are 
“reasonable”. The City already has language in the standards similar to AT&T’s request.  
 
o Design Standards, provision #17, the City does specify that “cables and 
connectors … must be routed underground to the extent feasible…” 
o Provision #17 “To the extent feasible, undergrounded cables and wires must 
transition directly into the pole base without any external doghouse. 
 
That being said, and to respond to AT&T’s comments, the City does agree to edit the City’s 
Standards of Approval, #16 “Underground Utilities” to add the underlined language below as 
follows: 
 
“16. Underground Utilities. In the event that other public utilities or cable television 
operators in the public right-of-way underground their facilities where the permittee’s 
wireless facility is located, the permittee must underground its equipment except the antennas

City of San Mateo 
June 7, 2021 
Page 8 of 8 
 
and antenna supports to the extent technically feasible. Such undergrounding shall occur at 
the permittee’s sole cost and expense except as reimbursed pursuant to law.” 
 
 
• Electric Meters. One of the city’s conditions of approval requires providers to swap out 
electric meters in the middle of a permit term under certain circumstances. Once AT&T 
installs a separate or ground-mounted electric meter, the facility is permitted to remain 
for the permit term. 
 
The City believes this comment is in reference to Standard Condition of Approval #17. This 
is required by the Building Code and is out of the City’s hands. 
 
347 of 445

--- page 348 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO RESPONSE TO VERIZON LETTER DATED JUNE 7, 2021 
 
Draft Standards (pp. 4-6) 
1. Least obtrusive. Despite a revision striking a reference to installations in other 
jurisdictions, this criterion is still problematic. The vague “least obtrusive” criteria could 
be used to deny small cells that otherwise satisfy the City’s more specific design 
standards. Such denials frustrate staff in their faithful implementation of the standards, as 
well as applicants, and also “materially inhibit” service improvements in violation of 
federal law. This item should be deleted. 
 
The provision reads the “least obtrusive possible”, and the City does specify that this is with 
regards to “appearance, size, and location”. This provision is intended to instruct the applicant to 
take into consideration the obtrusiveness and impact of the proposed wireless installation, and to 
the extent possible, try to minimize its impact in terms of size, appearance and location. The City 
does not believe that this provision in any way would “materially inhibit” service improvements 
in violation of federal law. 
 
That being said, and to respond to Verizon’s comments, the City revised its design standards as 
follows (new language indicated with underline), to clarify that an application will not be denied 
solely because it is not the least obtrusive: 
 
1. Least Obtrusive.  Proposed installations should be the least obtrusive possible in 
compliance with the City’s Design Standards, with regard to appearance, size, and 
location.   If installations are available that are less obtrusive than those allowed by the 
City’s wireless communications facilities ordinance, applicants must use those 
installations unless the City determines that those installations are not feasible. 
 
 
2. Pole heights minimized. While this provision properly acknowledges the antenna 
separation distance above utility pole conductors required by General Order 95, it also 
must allow for the antenna itself. Further, pole-top antennas may be placed on utility 
poles that do not carry electricity and are not subject to General Order 95. The phrase 
should be revised to read: “…four feet, plus the minimum separation from supply lines 
required by CPUC General Order 95….” 
 
The City made the following changes in response to Verizon’s comment (new language 
indicated with underline): 
 
2. Pole heights shall be minimized. The maximum height of any facilities mounted on an 
existing utility pole shall not exceed 4 feet, plus the minimum separation from supply 
lines required by CPUC General Order 95 (GO 95), as may be amended or superseded. 
Legally required lightning arresters and beacons shall be included when calculating the 
height of facilities. For wireless facilities placed on any new freestanding poles, the pole 
height, inclusive of all equipment, shall not exceed 35 feet.   
 
 
348 of 445

--- page 349 ---
13. Antennas. This provision requires placement of antennas at the top of a pole. 
However, for utility poles that carry electricity, antennas may be mounted to the middle 
of the pole in the communication utility zone, below electric conductors. If a mid-mount 
is required per General Order 95 or PG&E safety rules, then mandating a pole-top 
antenna would be technically infeasible and unreasonable. This provision must be 
revised to accommodate mid-mount antennas on utility poles. The utility pole standards 
(pp. 21-22) should be revised accordingly. 
 
The City made the following edits in response to Verizon’s comment (new language indicated 
with underline): 
 
13. Antenna Locations.  To the maximum extent technically feasible, antennas shall be 
placed at the top of the pole and shall be shrouded to the maximum extent feasible. 
 
The City also added the following provisions regarding antennas:  
 
14. Shrouding. All antennas and associated cables, jumpers, wires, mounts, masts, 
brackets and other connectors and hardware shall be installed within a single shroud or radome 
to the maximum extent technically feasible. For wireless facilities on utility poles, all antennas 
shall be shrouded, and located at the top of the pole or strand-mounted, to the maximum extent 
technically feasible. For pole-top antennas, the shroud shall not exceed 2.5 times the median pole 
diameter and must taper down to pole. For side-arm antennas, the shroud shall cover the cross 
arm and any cables, jumpers, wires or other connectors between the vertical riser and the 
antenna. 
 
15. Antenna Volume. Each individual antenna associated with a single wireless facility 
shall not exceed 3 cubic feet. The cumulative volume for all antennas on a single wireless facility 
shall not exceed: (A) 3 cubic feet in residential areas; or (B) 6 cubic feet in nonresidential areas. 
 
16. Horizontal Projection. Side-mounted antennas, where permitted, shall not project: (A) 
more than 2 feet from the support structure; (B) over any roadway for vehicular travel; or (C) 
over any abutting private property. If applicable laws require a side-mounted antenna to project 
more than 2 feet from the support structure, the projection shall be no greater than required for 
compliance with such laws. Pole-mounted antennas and accessory equipment should be installed 
in a manner that minimizes horizontal projections, which includes, without limitation, installing 
equipment within the pole or as flush to the pole as possible. 
 
 
17. Utilities. The City cannot forbid new aerial lines where there are already lines 
attached to a utility pole. An additional line is not “out-of-character” among existing 
aerial lines, and the standard would be unreasonable according to the FCC. This attempts 
to regulate the electric and communication backhaul lines belonging to other companies, 
installed under separate franchises or permits. The City should allow new aerial lines 
where already attached to utility poles. 
 
349 of 445

--- page 350 ---
The City does not have an outright prohibition on new aerial lines. Instead, the City requires that 
cables be routed underground “to the extent feasible”.  
 
That being said, the City made the following changes (new language indicated with underline): 
 
20. Utilities. All cables and connectors for telephone, primary electric and other similar 
utilities shall be routed underground to the extent feasible in conduits large enough to 
accommodate future collocated wireless facilities. To the extent feasible, undergrounded cables 
and wires must transition directly into the pole base without any external doghouse. Meters, 
panels, disconnect switches and other associated improvements must be placed in inconspicuous 
locations to the extent possible and technically feasible. The City shall not approve new 
overhead utility lines or service drops merely because compliance with the undergrounding 
requirements would increase the project cost. In areas with existing overhead lines, new 
communication lines shall be “overlashed” with existing communication lines to the extent 
technically feasible. Microwave or other wireless backhaul is discouraged when it would involve 
a separate and unconcealed antenna. 
 
Application Requirements 
Project purpose and technical objectives. This information bears no relation to the 
required findings and standards for approval right-of-way facilities. Further, the City 
cannot require such information regarding need because Public Utilities Code Section 
7901 grants telephone corporations such as Verizon Wireless a statewide right to place 
their equipment along any right-of-way. The FCC determined that small cells are needed 
to enhance service, introduce new services and densify networks, which are Verizon Wireless’s 
objectives in placing small cells in San Mateo. The FCC also disfavored dated service standards 
based on “coverage gaps” and the like, so the coverage/capacity information, propagation maps, 
drive test data and network maps sought by this submittal requirement are preempted.3 This 
submittal requirement should be deleted. 
 
There is nothing in the FCC small cell order, or in Public Utilities Code Section 7901 that 
preempts the City from requiring information regarding the proposed project’s technical service 
objectives and the nature of existing service capabilities. Although the FCC may disfavor 
“decisions that have applied solely a ‘coverage gap’- based approach under Section 
332(c)(7)(B)(i)(II)” (see FCC Order ¶ 40), this does not mean that the FCC intended to limit a 
city’s access to such information or prohibit any consideration of existing coverage. Many other 
cities also require such similar information. 
 
That being said, and to respond to Verizon’s comments, the City has edited the Design Standards 
to remove the requirements in Appendix B regarding propagation maps. 
 
 
Alternative site analysis. If a proposed small cell is in the most-preferred location according to 
the Design Standards’ site location preferences, there is no reason to analyze alternatives. As 
noted, propagation maps are inapplicable to small cells in the right-of way, and should not be 
required. This item should be revised so an alternatives analysis 
350 of 445

--- page 351 ---
is not required if siting in a preferred location, and the reference to propagation maps should be 
deleted. 
 
There is nothing in the FCC order or applicable federal law that prohibits the City from requiring 
such an alternative site analysis. 
 
As discussed above, the City deleted the requirements in Appendix B regarding propagation 
maps.

SUBJECT:
Transition to Elections By-District – Updated Schedule
RECOMMENDATION:
Adopt a Resolution to approve an updated schedule to transition to district-based elections and authorize staff to make 
adjustments, as needed, to the schedule.
BACKGROUND:
On May 24, 2021, the City received a certified letter from attorney Scott Rafferty asserting that the City’s current at-large 
election system may violate the California Voting Rights Act and stating the City should voluntarily move to a district-based 
election system for electing City Council members. On June 21, 2021, the City Council adopted Resolution No. 69 (2021), 
stating the City’s intention to transition from at-large to by-district elections. Appended to that Resolution was an exhibit 
outlining two tentative timelines for the required public hearing schedule. The first schedule showed the estimated timing 
for transitioning to by-district elections within 90 days from adopting the resolution of intention per Elections Code Section 
10010.  This initial 90-day period ends on September 20, 2021. The second timeline incorporated a 90-day extension, if an 
agreement could be reached with Mr. Rafferty, and as authorized by Elections Code Section 10010. 
This is a seminal change to how voters elect their City Council members and the City has stated publicly its commitment to 
a robust public outreach and its interest in using the latest 2020 US Census data. 
90-Day Timing Scenario 
Under the 90-day timing scenario, the City would have to complete the entire process of holding five public hearings to 
receive input from communities of interest, solicit map drawing from the public, and have a discussion regarding election 
sequencing all completed by September 20, 2021. This timeline would significantly limit the City’s ability to engage the 
community, particularly hard-to-reach underrepresented community members, through additional outreach 
opportunities. 
Furthermore, under this scenario, the City would not be able to use the latest 2020 US Census data, which is scheduled to 
be released in late September 2021, just days after the September 20, 2021 deadline. The City would then need to 
immediately begin a redistricting process, thereby having to conduct a second series of public hearings and outreach to 
adjust the district maps to align with the 2020 Census data in time for elections in November 2022. This would result in 
significant additional financial expenses and would likely be confusing to the public. 
Preferred Extended Timing Scenario 
Instead, the City sought to use a 90-day extension in order to use updated 2020 Census data and engage in a significantly 
more robust public outreach campaign. The City would conduct one set of public hearings (five in all), host at least three 
additional community workshops, attend numerous pop-up events, and hold significant outreach meetings with service 
352 of 445

--- page 353 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
organizations and neighborhoods to ensure we are receiving input from all of the community’s stakeholders. This allows 
the City to draw maps once, using the most current census data. The process culminates with the adoption of an ordinance 
outlining the election sequencing and the official district map to be used for the next decade. 
The City has negotiated at length with Mr. Rafferty for a 90-day extension agreement to complete the transition to district-
based elections.  However, these negotiations have been unsuccessful.  Nevertheless, the City is proposing using the 
second extended timing scenario, with a hearing schedule that would complete the process by November 15, 2021 
through the adoption of an ordinance transitioning to district elections. This allows approximately two more months of 
robust public outreach through multiple avenues of public input and use of the most current Census data to draw district 
maps that best reflect the community of San Mateo. This schedule would also not require a full 90-day extension.  
It is recommended that the City adopt the proposed resolution that includes Exhibit A, which outlines the public hearing 
schedule with proposed additional public outreach opportunities to be undertaken and authorizes staff to make 
adjustments to the schedule as needed. 
BUDGET IMPACT:
There is no additional budget impact from this administrative action.
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
This approval of public hearing and community outreach schedule is not a project subject to CEQA, because it is an 
organizational or administrative activity that will not result in direct or indirect physical changes in the environment. (CEQA 
Guidelines Section 15378(b)(5).)
NOTICE PROVIDED
All meeting noticing requirements were met.
ATTACHMENTS
Att 1 - Proposed Resolution with Exhibit A – District Schedule

CITY OF SAN MATEO 
RESOLUTION NO. ____ (2021) 
 
APPROVAL OF UPDATED PROPOSED TIMEFRAME TO TRANSITION FROM AT-LARGE TO BY-DISTRICT ELECTIONS 
UNDER ELECTIONS CODE SECTION 10010 AND TENTATIVE PUBLIC OUTREACH SCHEDULE 
 
 
WHEREAS, on June 21, 2021, in response to a letter from attorney Scott Raffety regarding City 
compliance with the California Voting Rights Act, the Council adopted Resolution No. 69 (2021) declaring an 
intent to transition from at-large to by-district council member elections under Elections Code Section 10010.  
The Resolution included a tentative calendar with two different timing scenarios; and 
 
 
WHEREAS, Elections Code Section 10010 provides for an initial 90 days from the adoption of a resolution 
of intent to accomplish the public process to move from at-large elections to by-district elections, and allows for 
an extension of an additional 90 days in order to provide additional time to conduct public outreach, encourage 
public participation, and receive public input; and 
 
WHEREAS, 2020 U.S. Census data is scheduled to be released in late September 2021, and the City 
would be required to use this data to draw its district maps.  However, the initial 90-day timeframe ends before 
the release of up-to-date Census data; and 
 
WHEREAS, having to immediately redraw district boundaries using 2020 Census data following the 
completion of the transition to district elections would be duplicative, confusing and costly; and  
 
 
WHEREAS, the City desires to use additional time to conduct a more comprehensive public process that 
also incorporates 2020 U.S. Census data in a single process that will utilize five public hearings along with robust 
public outreach to garner significant input from the community; and 
 
 
WHEREAS, the City fully intends to complete the transition under the CVRA within the extended 
timeline, and establish district boundaries no later than six months before its next Regular Election on 
November 8, 2022, consistent with Elections Code Section 10010(e)(3)(c)(i); and  
 
 
WHEREAS, the City’s proposed timeline, attached to this resolution as Exhibit A and incorporated by 
reference, outlines a process in which an ordinance transitioning the City to by-district elections and approving a 
district map would be adopted by November 15, 2021. 
 
 
NOW, THEREFORE, THE CITY COUNCIL OF THE CITY OF SAN MATEO, CALIFORNIA, HEREBY RESOLVES 
that:  
 
1. Exhibit A to this resolution entitled City of San Mateo Districting Proposed Timelines and Public 
Outreach Schedule is hereby approved.

SUBJECT:
Downtown Surplus Properties – Selection of Development Team
RECOMMENDATION:
Direct staff to negotiate an Exclusive Negotiating Agreement with the Prometheus/Harvest Properties/Alta Housing 
development team to explore the development of affordable housing on the City-owned parking lot located at East 4th 
Avenue and South Railroad Avenue (Talbot’s parking lot) and reject the other proposals submitted for the East 4th Avenue 
and South Railroad Avenue and for the 308 East 5th Avenue (Ravioli parking lot) sites. 
BACKGROUND:
The City received inquiries from real estate developers interested in developing two surface parking lots Downtown 
including the 57-space Talbot’s lot directly behind the former Talbot’s store accessed at E. 4th Avenue and S. Railroad 
Avenue and the 40-space Ravioli House lot off of B Street between 5th and 6th Avenues. In order to explore future 
redevelopment opportunities on these sites, the City has to comply with the State’s Surplus Lands Act and first entertain 
proposals for affordable housing development on the properties before considering any other possible use.
City Council approved a resolution declaring the properties as “Surplus Land” at the January 4, 2021 City Council meeting. 
On January 7, 2021, staff released a Notice of Surplus Lands Availability to all housing developers that have registered with 
the State Department of Housing and Community Development (HCD). The Notice required interested parties to submit a 
letter of interest to develop affordable housing by March 8, 2021.  The City received 4 proposals in response to this letter 
from the following development teams:

Prometheus/Harvest Properties/Alta Housing (Talbots lot site)

MidPen Housing (Talbots lot site)

Mercy Housing (Talbots lot site)

West Development Ventures (both the Talbots and Ravioli lot sites)
The City Council reviewed the proposals during a Closed Session discussion on April 19, 2021. At the meeting, the 
Council directed staff to reject the proposal from West Development Ventures for developing the Talbot’s and Ravioli sites 
jointly since the proposal was for only 17 units across both properties, which is well below what would be considered the 
highest and best use of the sites. The City Council directed staff to put plans for the redevelopment of the Ravioli site on 
hold until it could be evaluated for rezoning, potentially as part of the General Plan process. Council directed staff to 
continue negotiations with the 3 remaining submitters for the Talbot’s site. The Council requested that the submitters 
revise their initial proposals to respond to the following parameters and Council questions:
1)
Develop an 100% affordable housing project featuring a range of unit sizes from studios up to 3-bedrooms. Project 
should accommodate a mix of incomes and age ranges.
2)
Maximize the number of units that can be accommodated on the site by building a 7-story project.
356 of 445

--- page 357 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
3)
Provide a ground floor space of approximately 5,000 square feet that can be used by the non-profit Self-Help for 
the Elderly. It can be assumed that they can contribute some amount to rent, but not likely a market rent, such as 
would be charged to a retailer. They will also provide some funding for their own tenant improvements.
4)
The City will contribute the land for the project in the form of a long-term ground lease.
5)
Specify the expected ask of financial assistance for the affordable housing from the City. It is expected that the 
development team will minimize the City’s financial assistance to the best possible extent by applying for multiple 
outside sources of financing.
6)
Indicate if the development team would consider trying to acquire the parcels immediately fronting the Talbot’s 
parking lot along B Street for a larger development site.
7)
Indicate if the development team would consider entering into a Project Labor Agreement for this project.
All three of the remaining submitters revised their proposals to accommodate these proposed project changes. A matrix 
comparing the details of the revised submittals is included as Attachment 1 to this report. Copies of the revised 
submissions as well as the original submission for each proposer are included as Attachments 2 through 5 of this report. 
Evaluation of Proposals
As a result of the parameters provided, the 3 submissions are similar in content. All are for 100% affordable housing 
projects providing a mix of unit sizes and serving a variety of household types. Additionally, all of the development teams 
were willing to provide space for the non-profit Self Help for the Elderly on the ground floor of the building. At the June 21, 
2021 Closed Session discussion, the City Council gave direction to staff on the key inputs to determine which team to 
select for exclusive negotiations for the Talbot’s parking lot site.
Although there are variations in the numbers of units proposed, each team did a very preliminary analysis of the 
development potential of the site for the purpose of the proposal. Staff believes that once more detailed architectural 
analysis has been completed and all applicable City zoning and design standards are applied, including required site set-
backs, that the numbers of units are likely to change and would be similar across all proposals. In addition, although the 3 
projects varied in the amount of additional affordable housing assistance they estimated they would need from the City, 
they will be accessing the same competitive outside financing sources and that the amount of City funding needed would 
be end up being similar for all of the proposals.
A critical factor that staff evaluated was the willingness and/or ability of the development teams to acquire adjacent 
properties. The Talbot’s parking lot is a small constrained site and is adjacent to parcels containing one-story retail 
buildings. If redeveloped into a 7-story building, as proposed, the transition in height between the development and the 
adjacent buildings would be hard to mitigate. The Prometheus/Harvest Properties/Alta Housing development team is 
already in contract for the former Talbot’s Toy Store property and has initiated efforts to acquire the other two properties 
on the block. The inclusion of additional parcels may result in the ability to provide more affordable housing units on the 
site as well as providing more parking spaces, which will offset the loss of redeveloping the public parking lot.
Another important factor for consideration was the affordability target of each of the proposals. Two of the three 
proposals include units targeting households up to 80% of Area Median Income (AMI) whereas the MidPen Housing 
proposal capped out at 60% AMI.  The Council indicated an interest in serving as broad a range of incomes as possible, 
including those approaching middle-income. In order to qualify for affordable housing financing, all of the projects would 
be required to achieve the same average level of affordability so the higher income units will be balanced by the provision 
of more very-low income units, which will achieve the same depth of affordability but serve a wider-range of income 
levels.
Selection of Development Team
Based on the evaluation of the revised proposals, staff recommends that the City Council direct staff to negotiate an 
Exclusive Negotiating Agreement (ENA) with the Prometheus/Harvest Properties/Alta Housing team to explore the 
redevelopment of the Talbot’s parking lot site in conjunction with other properties on the same block. Staff recommends 
that the City reject the remaining proposals for the Talbot’s parking lot site. The ENA would commit the City to not explore 
other development opportunities for the property during the time that the development team is pursuing project 
357 of 445

--- page 358 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
entitlements and negotiating the deal terms for a future Disposition and Development Agreement. The ENA would also 
commit the development team to a schedule of performance to ensure they are doing their due diligence and acting in a 
timely manner to move towards the redevelopment of the site. Staff plans to bring back the ENA for City Council approval 
in September.
BUDGET IMPACT:
The future disposition of the Talbot’s Parking lot site will not result in any revenue for the City since the land will be 
contributed to support the development of affordable housing on the site. In addition, commitment of other City 
affordable housing funds of approximately $2 million is anticipated to support the financing of the project.
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
This action is not a project subject to CEQA, because negotiating an Exclusive Negotiating Agreement to explore the terms 
for the potential disposition of property is not a project which may result in a potentially significant physical impact on the 
environment. (Public Resources Code Section 21065.) A future Disposition and Development Agreement for development 
on the property will be subject to CEQA.  
NOTICE PROVIDED
All meeting noticing requirements were met.

RE: 
Notice of Availability of Surplus Land – Response to Follow-up Proposal Parameters

Dear Ms. Kleinbaum: 
Thank you for the opportunity to respond to your questions and comments as described in the “Surplus 
Land’s Act Follow-up Proposal Parameters,” related to the surplus land owned by the City of San Mateo, 
located at the corner of S. Railroad Avenue and E. 4th Avenue in Downtown San Mateo (“Talbots City

Please consider the below response as supplemental information to the original proposal submitted on 
March 8, 2021, and the response to follow-up questions, provided on April 5, 2021.  As previously 
expressed, the Harvest Properties, Prometheus and Alta Housing team remain enthusiastic and 
dedicated to working collaboratively with you, your staff, and all City of San Mateo departments to 
advance the objectives and priorities of the City with respect to the site, which offers a unique 
opportunity to create a much needed supply of affordable housing for San Mateo’s residents as well as 
energize and beautify this part of Downtown San Mateo. 
 
In the following narrative, you will find a detailed response to each question posed.  Before delving into 
these details, we wanted to highlight four key elements or components of our proposed plan for your 
review and consideration.   
 
Project Vision:  Alignment with City Objectives 
Our development team is committed to providing a 100% affordable housing project on the Talbots City 
Lot that will feature a range of unit sizes from studios to 3-bedroom units, and will maximize the number 
of units that the site can accommodate in a 7-story project.  We understand that the City wants the 
project to accommodate a wide mix of income levels and age ranges, and can assure you that we share 
this as a critical and essential priority. 
 
As we mentioned in our April response letter to the City, if a Seniors residential community were the 
preferred direction, based on a unit mix that includes all studio and 1-bedroom units, the preliminary 
design for the site yields approximately 80 units of housing. This is a viable option that our development 
team would be very interested and open to pursuing in collaboration with the City.  We are equally 
committed to developing a Family-oriented project, if the City opted to move in that direction.  Based on 
our current design and yield analysis, a Family-oriented project would generate 52-54 housing units, and 
house 138 residents (according to HUD guidelines).  In this development scenario, the unit mix would 
consist of over 50% of the units as two and three-bedrooms and the income levels would range from 
30% to 80% AMI, with an average income level of approximately 43%. 
360 of 445

--- page 361 ---
Our team has a shared commitment to provide our residents of all ages with services which are 
resident-drive, strength-based, impactful, and empowering.  As you can see in the attached Site Plan 
Exhibit, located on the second story (at the podium level), our plan will include interior and exterior 
amenity areas for the residents to use and enjoy including a tot lot for children.  While the programming 
and design details have not yet been developed, we anticipate that these spaces including community 
space on the ground level will be utilized for a full range of family-oriented activities, including but not 
limited to: flexible spaces for after-school educational programs, play equipment and areas for children 
to exercise and socialize, food security and health and wellness programs, and recreational space for the 
community to meet and gather.  
 
Additionally, at the ground level, there will be a welcoming entrance and lobby area for families to enter 
the building and feel a sense of privacy and comfort.  Again, while the specific details and interior space 
planning will be developed over time with the input of the community and the City, we anticipate that 
this area will include a welcome desk, resident mailbox area, seating areas, package room, and a 
community notice or bulletin board, and community gathering area. The ground floor retail area will be 
leased long-term to Self-Help for the Elderly, which will include a large multi-purpose area. Given that a 
Family-oriented project generally has most of its events in the late afternoon and evening, the 
development team hopes to work with Self-Help for the Elderly to allow the residential community to 
have controlled access to this space during non-business hours.  
 
Self-Help for the Elderly 
On the ground floor of the proposed plan, our development team is committed to providing space to 
house, the non-profit Self-Help for the Elderly.  We had the great pleasure of meeting with Ms. Anni 
Chung, its President and CEO. We applaud the work of this non-profit organization, that has tirelessly 
strived and succeeded in serving seniors in the community for over 50 years.  We would be delighted to 
work with Self-Help for the Elderly to support its efforts towards improving the quality of life for older 
adults by empowering themselves and providing a comprehensive range of multicultural and 
multilingual services.   
 
Our current site plan has approximately 5,000 sq. ft. of ground floor community-serving space.  In our 
meeting with Ms. Chung, she shared more about Self-Help for the Elderly’s specific space needs for their 
programs.  Specifically, they need office space for health care-related supportive and administrative 
services, a small prep kitchen, as well as a multi-purpose room that can be used to provide meal service 
(65-75 people), classroom/workshop space, and performance and recreational uses. Based on these 
requirements, the development team will be able to meet all of their needs to allow them to continue 
providing the desired services for the community at the project.  
 
Additionally, since our team has site control of the adjacent property at S. B. Street, we believe we can 
expand the development footprint, and can provide an even more robust set of community benefits, 
which could also include additional ground floor space for Self-Help for the Elderly to ensure that their 
current and future needs could be satisfied.  We understand the limited resources and financial 
constraints of this non-profit organization and their importance to the whole San Mateo community.  
Our team plans to provide this space at an extremely below market rent.  The team will work with Self-
Help for the Elderly to ensure an appropriate build-out that meets their vision for the space. In our 
361 of 445

--- page 362 ---
discussions with Self-Help for the Elderly, between our delivery condition and their reserved funds, we 
collectively will be able to provide the required capital for them to successfully move into the project.  
 
Outside Sources of Funding 
Our development team understands the City’s proposed structure, which consists of the City’s 
contribution of the Talbots City Lot, in the form of a long-term ground lease for $1 per year. That said, 
the development team prefers a fee simple transfer, but can accommodate the ground lease structure, 
if preferred by the City.   Additionally, we fully appreciate the importance of minimizing the City’s 
financial assistance to the maximum extent possible by applying for multiple outside sources of funding.   
In response to this City priority, we have explored, researched and analyzed the outside sources as 
detailed in the “Follow-up Proposal Parameters”, and have determined that a Family-oriented project of 
our size and scope would qualify for and be able to obtain these outside funding sources.   
 
Project Labor Agreement  
The development team shares the City’s priority for supporting local Labor Unions, wherever possible.  
The Partnership is open to exploring a potential Project Labor Agreement for this project. In that spirit, 
the project team has reached out to the SMC Building Trades Council to initiate that discussion and we 
look forward to continuing the dialogue on this topic.

have any further questions or comments, our development team would be very happy to answer any 
questions you might have.  As we have stated in both our original proposal and our previous response to

and attractive affordable housing for its residents.    
 
We are confident that if the City were to choose our development team, you would find the Alta

stakeholders.  Harvest and Prometheus are very proud to be working with Alta Housing on this

non-profit has been developing and managing affordable housing in San Mateo and Santa Clara Counties 
for over 50 years.  They are uniquely positioned to manage and provide all the residential supportive 
care services that will be needed and required for the residents to thrive. 
 
Working collaboratively with Self Help for the Elderly, in conjunction with the City of San Mateo, we are

If you should have any further questions or require additional clarification, please do not hesitate to let

City Question Responses:  
1) How many units do you propose to build? 
The project currently proposes 52-54-Family-oriented units including a mix of studios, one-bedroom, 
two-bedroom, and three-bedroom units. Please see below chart for a detailed breakdown of the 
unit mix. The Family-oriented project continues to be our recommended approach as the project 
scores very strong for many funding sources, caters to residents of all ages creating a multi-
generational project, and despite fewer total units, the project will accommodate 138-residents 
which is approximately 25% more residents than a 90-unit senior housing project. The development 
team has elected to provide a larger amenity area on the second floor that creates an 
indoor/outdoor environment resulting in a 52-unit project, but if the City prefers the higher range of 
units then the development team can reduce the size of the indoor amenity area to add two 
additional units, resulting in a 54-unit project.  
 
 
 
2)  What income ranges would your project accommodate? 
The project provides units ranging from 30% to 80% AMI with an average income level of 
approximately 43%.  
 
3)  How much on-site parking are you able to provide for the residential units? 
The project currently proposes 90 total parking stalls across the site. The current plan for the Family-
oriented housing project is to allocate 0.7 stalls per unit (37-stalls) to the residential portion of the 
project. The four different uses at the project have different peak demand times. The goal will be to 
incorporate a shared parking concept, as is standard in Downtown San Mateo projects, among the 
different uses: i) residential, ii) commercial, iii) retail, and iv) public parking. This shared concept will 
provide excess parking stalls to the residential units during off-peak hours.  If additional parcels on 
the block are acquired, then it will allow for an increased below-grade parking structure providing 
the opportunity for additional stalls to be allocated to the residential as well as additional flex 
parking for the residential and the public during off-peak commercial times.   
 
4)  Describe any on-site amenities associated with your project.  
The project will dedicate the majority of the ground floor to Self-Help for the Elderly to provide a 
permanent location for the organization so it can continue to serve the needs of the community of 
San Mateo.  Self-Help for the Elderly’s space will consist of a multi-faceted large community room to 
serve daily lunches, host dancing/music and other recreational events, as well as allow for the 
option of partitioning the space into three classroom areas for educational workshops. The 
development team also plans to work with Self-Help for the Elderly so that some of this space may 
be available to the residential community in the evenings and on the weekends.  We see these two 
uses as being very complimentary to each other.

The second floor of the project will provide 1,500 square feet of indoor space and over 2,500 square 
feet of outdoor space to create an indoor/outdoor amenity area for the residents. This space has 
not been fully programmed but will consist of flexible spaces for after-school activities and 
homework, play equipment and areas for children to exercise and socialize, and recreational space 
for the community to meet and gather. 
 
The development team is committed to developing a community where residents not only live but 
thrive.  Alta Housing has a long track record of providing on-site supportive services to its residential 
communities.  Alta Housing’s mission has been to partner with residents to provide services which 
are resident-driven, strength-based, impactful, and empowering.  Alta currently provides support in 
the following areas: 1) Supporting Counseling & Housing Retention to help residents meet their 
goals, 2) Community Building & Resident Leadership, and 3) Educational Programs & Social Services 
such as after-school and summer youth programs, parenting classes, financial literacy workshops, 
job coaching, and health and wellness initiatives.  
 
5) What is your expectation of the required ask of the City’s affordable housing fund? 
The revised proposal requests $2,000,000 of funding from the City of San Mateo. The development 
team will exhaust all potential avenues of funding prior to relying on the City for any financial 
commitment. The current proposal does not include the Commercial Linkage fee from the 
development team’s commercial projects. The Commercial Linkage fee will be paid directly to the 
City for each project. The Commercial Linkage fee will increase the City’s affordable housing fund by 
over 50%, providing the ability for the City to apply these funds to additional housing opportunities, 
thereby helping to address the housing shortage within the Bay Area. Based on current available 
funding sources, we estimate the $5.6M of Commercial Linkage fees could be leveraged to create an 
additional 20-30 housing units for the community.  
 
The $5.6M of proposed Commercial Linkage fees that will be paid to the City includes $1,300,000 of 
additional fees that is directly related to the Talbots City Lot that the City would not obtain if the 
team is unsuccessful in acquiring a lease-hold interest in the City Lot. Additionally, the development 
team will contribute equity up to $2,900,000 as a Developer Community Benefit Contribution to the 
project to cover any potential shortfall and provide for greater certainty of execution.   
 
6) What other funding sources will you seek for this project? 
With funding for affordable housing currently and projected to be in very short supply, our team 
worked diligently and engaged a third-party expert to confirm the potential competitiveness of the 
full range of state, federal, and other housing funding programs (see table below).  In this way and 
combined with our own Developer Community Benefit Contribution, we aim to minimize and 
potentially eliminate financial support necessary from the City. Please see below the competitive 
funding scoring matrix developed for our project:

Furthermore, our due diligence confirms that the proposed project design and location meets the 
current regulatory requirements necessary to compete as a family project in a High Resource Area, 
giving it a distinct competitive advantage to all other projects across the State which do not meet 
these requirements. 
 
At the same time, our financing plan takes into account that two sources which continue to be 
challenging or out-of-reach for even the best family projects, namely Section 8 vouchers and 9% tax 
credits.  
• 
For Section 8, our due diligence confirmed that currently project-based vouchers are 
oversubscribed with limited availability except for an allocation for veterans. We will continue to 
stay in contact with the County should that change. 
• 
The 9% tax credit program continues to be limited due to the size of the Region. That said, we 
are looking at maximizing the adaptability to be competitive for 9% and understand that HCD is 
exploring the possibility of allowing the MHP program to be used with 9% tax credits; this is 
currently disallowed.  Were that to happen, we would apply our full effort to achieve the 
9%/MHP combo, in order to reach the above-stated goal of reducing or eliminating City funding. 
 
Funding Sources Overview: 
Our projections effectively and fully leverage State funding and other sources – including the Developer 
Community Benefits Contribution – to minimize the City’s financial assistance.  A discussion of each 
source follows: 
a. 4% Tax Credit:  
The project assumes 4% tax credit funding with the competitive CDLAC tiebreaker necessary to 
obtain this funding.  
 
365 of 445

--- page 366 ---
b. San Mateo County Affordable Housing Fund (AHF):  
In order to limit the number of required sources, the proposal currently does not rely on funding 
from the County, but during the financing process the development team will leave “no stone 
left unturned” and will exhaust all potential funding sources. The Developers Community Benefit 
Contribution will supplant the customary necessary funding from the County of San Mateo.  
c. Federal Home Bank Affordable Housing Program (AHP): 
We have included $765,000 in AHP funding; per our research, the project surpasses the 
threshold required to be considered competitive for this funding source. Of note, a higher per-
unit AHP loan than what we are including significantly reduces application competitiveness 
under current regulations. 
d. Project Based Vouchers:  
Conversations with the County have confirmed that currently project-based Section 8 vouchers 
are very limited and prioritized for veterans. The Section 8 program is most often called upon to 
support the limited income available from tenants exiting homelessness. Rather than rely upon 
this scarce resource – and potentially delay project construction – we instead opted to leverage 
the higher incomes available from the 80% AMI units and supplement the shortfall with the 
Developers Community Benefit Contribution. However, it is important to note that this did not 
come at the expense of fully funding the service provision for the formerly homeless tenants 
which is a necessary component to help them stay housed; our budget includes 1 FTE Service 
Coordinator and 1 FTE Services Specialist. 
e. Other State Programs: 
a. Multi-Family Housing Program (MHP) 
Our project intentionally takes full advantage of MHP for the following reasons: 
1. It is routinely the most-funded State program with the greatest number of 
funding “rounds” per year with the greatest funding per round. 
2. We have designed the project to meet the CTCAC requirements for family 
housing. That, combined with the site’s location in a High Resource Area, 
allows the project to garner a greater per-unit MHP subsidy vs. if either of 
these were not the case. This advantage comes without damaging the 
project’s overall competitiveness for this resource. 
3. The project has a very competitive tiebreaker and we are confident in our 
ability to obtain this financing.  
b. Infill Infrastructure Grant (IIG) 
We propose to pair the MHP funding with the IIG program.  Our due diligence has 
confirmed that the project could score extremely well, based upon the updated 
regulations issued this month. The IIG program will be an excellent source to cover 
related construction-period costs such as utility connections and sidewalk 
improvements. Our IIG “self-score” shows a high degree of feasibility in obtaining this 
competitive resource. 
 
c. Transit Oriented Development Programs (TOD) 
Although our due diligence confirms that the project would score very well for TOD (290 
out of 300, whereas 265 was the minimum to be awarded in the previous round), we 
propose not to rely upon this funding source unless one of the following facts is 
changed: 
366 of 445

--- page 367 ---
1. The TOD Program isn’t reliably available. 
1. The previous round in 2020 was the first TOD “NOFA” since 2014. 
2. HCD has not announced plans for another TOD NOFA. 
3. In addition, TOD funding often lags other programs ($140 million for 
TOD in 2020 vs. $160 million for IIG in 2021, $195 million for MHP in 
2021, and $297 million for MHP in 2020). 
2. HCD guidelines prohibit “subsidy stacking” – one unit cannot benefit from 
both MHP and TOD funding (but IIG is exempt). 
3. The greater per-unit loan amount available to this project under the MHP 
program – described above – is not available under TOD for any project; 
therefore, the maximum possible award under TOD will always be less than 
the maximum MHP award for this project under current regulations. 
 
 
7. Would you consider trying to acquire the parcels immediately fronting the Talbot’s parking lot 
along B-Street for a larger development site? 
The development team is open to acquiring the parcels fronting the Talbot’s parking lot. The 
opportunity to transform the full City block will lead to a very exciting development that will 
further allow the team to curate a marque project that meets the City’s vision for the 
Downtown area. Acquiring the adjacent sites will provide optimal site flexibility, additional 
parking stalls, enhanced architectural design, increased programming to activate the public 
realm, and provide subsidized rent to offer community serving retail. The team currently 
controls the 445 S. B Street (former Talbots Toy Store), which provides many benefits to the City 
Lot such as: i) replacement of public parking, ii) on-site parking for the residential units, iii) 
Developer Community Benefit Contribution of $2.9M to cover the potential short-fall and 
provide a further the ability to provide subterranean parking. 
8. Would you consider entering into a Project Labor Agreement for this project? 
The development team is open to exploring a Project Labor Agreement for this project and has 
reached out to James Ruigomez at the SMC Building Trades Council to discuss the potential to work 
together on this project.
