Dear Ms. Kleinbaum: 
It is with great pleasure that we submit our offer for the surplus land owned by the City of San Mateo, located 
at the corner of S. Railroad Avenue and E. 4th Avenue in Downtown San Mateo (“Talbots City Lot”).  This site 
offers an exceptional opportunity in Downtown San Mateo to provide a much-needed supply of affordable 
housing for San Mateo’s residents, to energize, enhance, and beautify the public realm along key street 
corridors, and contribute to the architectural legacy of Downtown San Mateo with unique, world-class design 
that fits into the character of the Downtown. 
The purpose of this letter is to provide an overview of our development proposal for the Talbots City Lot.  The 
materials that follow this letter will provide additional information and detail for your consideration and 
review.  The letter is organized into six sections: Development Team; What Our Team Has Heard; Development 
Vision; Affordable Housing Options, Adjacent Site-Control Advantages; and, Development Deal Structure. 
Development Team 
Our proposed development team for the Talbots City Lot site is a partnership of Harvest Properties, 
Prometheus Real Estate Group, and Alta Housing– all locally-based and community-focused developers.  The 
team strongly believes in and is committed to the core principles of investing long-term in the San Mateo 
community and building long-lasting relationships with the City and community stakeholders.  Prometheus and 
Harvest have been active in the San Mateo market for over five decades and currently manage approximately 
320 residential units and 850,000 sq. ft. of office space in San Mateo, in addition to 354 residential units under 
development (including 29 affordable for-sale housing units).  Prometheus is currently working on the 303 
Baldwin site in Downtown San Mateo, which will create a high-quality, uniquely designed building with the goal 
of enhancing the public realm near the Downtown Train Station. 
Alta Housing has been committed to providing affordable housing in Silicon Valley since its inception over 50 
years ago with the help of local community leaders.  Known for innovative development, outstanding resident 
support and high-quality maintenance of its buildings, Alta Housing manages over 700 units to house more 
than 2,000 low-income residents across the Bay Area.  Prometheus and Alta have previously collaborated on 
other projects located along the Peninsula and are looking forward to joining forces with Harvest Properties for 
this special development opportunity in Downtown San Mateo. 
376 of 445

--- page 377 ---
What Our Team Has Heard 
The development team has spent considerable time listening to the objectives for the disposition of the 
Talbots City Lot site from the City Council, City Staff, and the community of San Mateo.  What we have heard 
from these meetings related to the site as well as to the future vision for Downtown San Mateo is as follows: 
 Maximize unit count 
 Replace existing parking on-site 
 Preference for ground lease of the land 
 Design a project that would enhance the retail and public realm experience 
 Provide best in-class architecture while respecting the established downtown environment 
Our development proposal is designed to meet the needs, priorities and objectives of the City and community 
of San Mateo.  We look forward to reviewing our design proposal with you as the selection process progresses 
and continuing to listen to the community and incorporate that essential feedback into the development 
proposal. 
 
Development Vision 
Our development team is looking forward to partnering and working collaboratively with the City and all 
community stakeholders and leaders to create a dynamic, mixed-use, 100% affordable housing development 
on the Talbots City Lot site.  As will be described below, there are two different housing options to consider for 
the programming and residential populations served by the proposed project.  The guiding principle, 
underlying both options, is to build on the extraordinary foundation of Downtown San Mateo, and continue to 
enhance and energize the Downtown neighborhood core. 
 
The Talbots City Lot site presents an opportunity to curate 
and provide a diverse array of community-facing, locally-
based services at the ground floor of the new development.  
Depending on the needs and wishes of the community, 
these services could include: a diversified mix of restaurant 
and food offerings, spaces to support the local arts 
community, flexible youth-oriented spaces, public art 
installations, and local music venues.  Coupled with world 
class architecture and creative designs as envisioned by our 
development proposal, these ground floor spaces will help 
to enliven and energize the public realm and streetscapes 
along S. Railroad Avenue and E. 4th Avenue and ultimately 
create a special destination for local residents and visitors 
to Downtown San Mateo. 
 
As will be described in greater detail below, Prometheus 
and Harvest also control the adjacent property along S. B. Street, which is contiguous to the Talbots City Lot 
site.  Therefore, as you will see in the architectural renderings that are attached, our development proposal is 
uniquely positioned to expand the development footprint of the Talbots City Lot site and create an even more 
377 of 445

--- page 378 ---
robust set of complimentary community benefits that extend to S. B. Street.  Our vision is to provide a 
diversified mixture of restaurant options, curated with arts and music venues, public art, and a rich landscaping 
palette to create a marquis destination for the community. Among other features, our current proposal carves 
back the building’s footprint away from the property’s boundary, thereby widening the pedestrian ground 
plane at the sidewalk and creating the opportunity to double or triple the public realm with beautiful 
landscaping and well-designed parklets.  In adherence with the standards of San Mateo Downtown 
Streetscape guidelines, our ultimate vision is to develop a functional and beautifully designed gathering space, 
with varied retail offerings, that everyone in the community can enjoy. 
 
This is our development team’s preliminary vision for this special block of Downtown San Mateo; we look 
forward to discussing and exploring these ideas and concepts with the City and community as we move 
forward in the review and selection process. 
 
Affordable Housing Options 
As is the case across much of the Bay Area and the Peninsula, many jurisdictions are facing a severe shortage 
of affordable housing to offer their residents.  Safe, sustainable, and healthy affordable housing options for 
everyone is essential for the City and our region in order to continue to thrive into the future.  Our team would 
be proud to be able to contribute to the needed supply of affordable housing in the City of San Mateo on the 
Talbots City Lot site and has created two different affordable housing options summarized below. 
 
Please note, each option is further described and detailed in our attached proposal.  Additionally, a working 
assumption that maximizing the amount of affordable housing on the Talbots City lot guided each alternative, 
which assumes the maximum bulk and massing allowed on the site when using the State Density Housing 
Bonus program.  Our team recognizes that there may be hybrid or alternative options and are open to 
exploring those with the City as discussions progress. 
 
• 
Option 1: Family-oriented housing 
o Unit count:  By designing the site to its maximum building envelope, in adherence to the City’s 
Zoning Code as well as utilizing the State Density Housing Bonus, a total gross building square 
footage of 60,000 sq. ft. (or 52 units) could be achieved. 
o Unit mix:  As a family-oriented project, at least 50% of the units will be 2 and 3 BR units, with 
the balance (50%) as 1BR units and Studios. 
o Community:  While the project will be geared toward families, there will also be units available 
to San Mateo’s senior population, creating an inter-generational community. 
o Assistance:  Additionally, up to 10% of the units could be set aside as supportive housing (IDD 
population). 
o AMI:  30-80% of Area Median Income with an average of 50% of AMI, aiming to serve the 
broadest segments of the affordable housing market. 
o Parking:  Approximately 90 parking stalls would be provided. 
 
• 
Option 2:  Senior housing 
o Unit count:  Similar to Option 1, the project has been designed to maximize the building 
envelope in adherence to the City’s Zoning Code as well as utilizing the State Density Housing 
378 of 445

--- page 379 ---
Bonus, with a total building square footage of 60,000 sq. ft.  With smaller unit size on average, 
the total unit count achieved is 60 units. 
o Unit mix:  As a housing project geared toward seniors, the unit mix would, on average, be 
smaller unit sizes: 25% (2 and 3 BRs); 75% (1BR and Studios). 
o Assistance:  As is typical for a Seniors housing project, it would be dependent on Voucher 
support from the County of San Mateo:  25% Veteran vouchers; 25% Section 8 Project-Based 
vouchers. 
o AMI:  50% of Area Median Income (AMI). 
o Parking:  Similar to Option 1, approximately 90 parking stalls would be provided. 
 
Initial Recommendation 
While our team is certainly open to discussing the two different options presented above, due to some 
challenges related to financing Senior housing (i.e., availability of Section 8 Project-Based vouchers), our 
team’s recommendation would be to pursue Option 1:  Family-oriented housing.  Unlike Option 2, Option 1’s 
financial viability would not be dependent on the voucher program.  Additionally, Option 1 meets the broadest 
segment of the community, as it provides housing with unit sizes and a unit mix suitable for San Mateo’s 
families as well as its senior population.  As described above, our proposed plan is able to accommodate 90 
parking stalls (nearly doubling what is currently onsite) for the future residents of our community as well as 
spaces to be shared with the public. 
 
That said, our ultimate goal is to find a solution that meets the combined needs of the community and the City.  
If the City’s preference is to maximize the unit count within the buildable envelope (as reflected in Option 2), 
the development team will work collaboratively with the City to identify creative ways to replace the more 
typical method of funding these projects with other sources of funding.  And, we continue to be open to 
alternative options that are more preferable to and will meet the objectives of the City, in terms of unit count, 
level of affordability and residential population served. 
 
Adjacent Site-Control Advantages 
Due to our team’s control of the adjacent site at S. B. Street, our team is uniquely positioned to create the 
most expansive and complimentary experience for this block in Downtown San Mateo.  The development team 
is working on two other new, mixed-use commercial development projects in Downtown San Mateo, in 
proximate to the Talbots City Lot site:  31-57 S. B Street and 668 E. 3rd Avenue.  With this proprietary 
collection of projects in the pipeline, our development proposal for the Talbots City Lot site is able to achieve 
far greater results than if the Talbots City Lot were developed as stand-alone project.  Additionally, due to the 
collective land holdings and project scale controlled by the development team, we can provide better 
construction cost efficiency resulting in more units to the community with less reliance on subsidies. 
 
Below is a list of the key advantages that our development proposal can offer to the City: 
 
Financial Contributions 
 
Generate $5.6 million in Commercial Linkage Fees to be used as a needed funding source for the 
affordable housing provided at the Talbots City Lot site.  These fees are generated by the three 
commercial projects that the development team is currently proposing and would nearly double the 
City’s current affordable housing funds.  For comparison purposes, the development team would be 
379 of 445

--- page 380 ---
required to build approximately 13 units of housing to equate to the $5.6 million in Commercial 
Linkage Fees; however our project is proposing to deliver 52-60 units.  This represents an offering of 
nearly 300-400% more when including the contribution of the Talbots City Lot. 
 
Generate approximately $25 million in impact fees to the City (from the three development sites) to 
help address budget deficits in the City of San Mateo. 
 
Increase in revenue to the City by approximately $1.5 million from the incremental increase in 
property taxes from the three properties.  This increased valuation and associated property tax 
increase provides a long-term source of funding or annuity for the City of San Mateo to be utilized for 
a variety of City needs (i.e., potential budget deficits, future affordable housing projects, etc.) 
 
Public Realm/Public Parking 
 
As part of the development project, upwards of 90 parking stalls will be provided with the availability 
of shared public parking.  This nearly doubles the existing supply of parking that is currently on the 
Talbots City Lot site.  If the Talbots City Lot site was developed as a stand-alone project, the current 
publicly accessible parking spaces on site would likely be eliminated and no longer available to provide 
needed parking downtown.  Additionally, the size of the Talbots City Lot site makes a stand-alone 
underground parking facility very inefficient and extremely expensive to construct. 
 
As was described in the Development Vision section above, the project will significantly enhance and 
energize the public realm along the project’s streetscape frontages on S. Railroad Avenue, E. 4th 
Street, as well as on S. B Street – all which will contribute to the execution of the City’s vision for S. B 
Street and its Downtown core. 
 
As a benefit to the community, the project will partially subsidize the retail space in order to provide 
community-serving retail such as a music venue, art studios/gallery, maker spaces, bookstores, and 
other retail uses that might not otherwise be financially viable. 
 
In collaboration with the City, community stakeholders and the local business community, the 
development team will implement a hyper-local retail strategy by identifying retail categories that are 
missing from the area in order to complement the current Downtown offerings. 
 
Development Deal Structure 
While the development team’s strong preference would be to take ownership of the Talbots City Lot site as a 
fee simple interest.  We are certainly open to exploring other structures with the City such as a ground lease 
structure, which could be discussed and analyzed during our continued discussions with the City.  The 
capitalization structure outlined in our current proposal consists of the City of San Mateo contributing the 
Talbots City Lot land to the development team entity as part of the development transaction.  It is the project 
team’s understanding that there is a profit-sharing agreement on the Talbots City Lot which results in the City 
only receiving 30% of any cash payment received.  Therefore, in-lieu of providing a monetary payment, the 
team has, in effect, utilized these proceeds to create additional units on-site to the full benefit of the 
community. 
 
In order to achieve the development vision, create the maximum number of affordable housing units onsite, 
and provide the unique array of community services and benefits, our current financial analysis results in a City 
financial subsidy, of approximately $2,350,000 to provide the needed funding sources to capitalize the project 
[please see a high-level summary of funding sources in our exhibit]. 
 
380 of 445

--- page 381 ---
In summary, our development team is excited and committed to working collaboratively with you, your staff, 
and all appropriate City of San Mateo departments in order to achieve the City’s vision for the Talbots City Lot 
site.  Due to our team’s breadth of knowledge our familiarity and direct development experience in San 
Mateo, and the unique advantage of owning the adjacent site and other development sites in Downtown San 
Mateo, we are confident that our development proposal for the Talbots City Lot will satisfy the City’s 
objectives now and for many years to come. 
 
If you should have any questions, please do not hesitate to contact any member of our development team.  
We look forward to the next steps of this process.

and Washington.

ALTA HOUSING
Alta Housing is an independent non-profit agency that builds, 
develops, acquires, and manages low- and moderate-income housing 
in San Mateo and Santa Clara Counties.  Alta Housing has been 
committed to providing affordable housing in the Bay Area since its 
inception over 50 years ago with the help of local community 
leaders. With a committed staff of over 50, Alta Housing is a Santa 
Clara County based 501(c)(3). 
Our Mission: To create stronger, more diverse communities by 
providing and maintaining high-quality affordable housing where 
individuals and families thrive.
Alta Housing provides high-quality, safe housing; subsidize rents to 
the greatest degree possible and support our residents in reaching 
their goals through the Resident Services program. Alta’s portfolio 
consists of over 700 residential units housing more than 2,000 low-
income residents. Alta’s properties are geared towards families, 
seniors and special needs populations. 
As a long-term developer and owner of affordable properties on the 
mid-peninsula, Alta Housing brings significant financial security and 
the ability to leverage creative funding sources to any new 
development project

1) How many units do you propose to build? 
We have asked our Architect to model 4 different scenarios:  
1a) Baseline 7-Story scenario where we preserve the trees on 4th Avenue 
1b) Baseline tree-preserving scenario where we add an eighth story 
2a) Alternate 7-Story scenario where we remove the trees and build to the property line 
2b) Alternate tree-removing scenario where we add an eighth story 
 
Below are the unit matrices for each scenario: 
 
We will solicit feedback from the City and the community to assess which scenario is preferred. 
The exercise of creating multiple scenarios allows us to weigh the tradeoffs. For example, 
retaining the mature trees on 4th Avenue reduces the unit count by 10, reduces parking stalls by 
6, and reduces the ground floor space for Self Help by 1200 square feet. Adding an extra story of 
housing increases the unit count by 10-12 homes.

--- page 404 ---
2 
 
2) What income ranges would your project accommodate? 
MidPen intends to serve households with incomes ranging from below 30% AMI up to 60% AMI. 
The targeting within the proposed income range is broad to ensure a spectrum of extremely and 
very low-income households qualify: 20% at 30% AMI (ELI), 20% at 40% AMI, 20% at 50% AMI, 
and the remaining 40% at 60% AMI. The average AMI across the community is expected at 
roughly 48% AMI. 
3) How much on-site parking are you able to provide for the residential units? 
We propose a reduced parking ratio for this prime, downtown, transit-oriented location. The 
reduced parking ratio prioritizes the ground-level street frontage for Self-Help for the Elderly’s 
Senior Center. Reduced parking also reduces project cost, for example by avoiding underground 
parking, and building complications such as ongoing maintenance of parking stackers.  
In Scenario 1a and 1b, where we preserve the trees on 4th Avenue, we can achieve 14 parking 
stalls, which results in a 0.25 parking ratio in the 7-story building and a 0.21 parking ratio in the 
8-story building. In Scenario 2a and 2b, where we remove the trees and build to the property 
line, we can achieve 20 parking stalls, which results in a 0.30 parking ratio in the 7-story building 
and a 0.26 parking ratio in the 8-story building. 
MidPen has experience managing reduced parking ratios. Transportation Demand Management 
for this development may include a combination of the following measures: 
• 
Car sharing space(s) on-site, with subsidized car sharing membership 
• 
Free or subsidized transit passes 
• 
Provision of ample bike storage, including bike repair space 
• 
No car preference 
• 
Marketing the development as transit-oriented during lease up 
• 
Marketing transit options through ongoing operations, for example through a transit 
hub within the building that provides a transit screen and/or schedules 
• 
Layering of special needs preferences, such as formerly homeless (less likely to have car) 
 
4) Describe any on-site amenities associated with your project. 
In addition to the community serving Self-Help for the Elderly Senior Center, the development 
will feature on-site amenities for residents only:  
• 
Property management office 
• 
Community room 
• 
Outdoor recreation space including play area 
• 
Laundry facilities 
• 
Secured bike storage 
 
MidPen would also study sharing resident services space with neighboring Kiku Crossing. A 
shared approach benefits residents by connecting them in a community that extends beyond 
their building. The shared approach also leverages the three resident service provider offices 
and large learning center at Kiku Crossing to maximize housing and community-serving space at 
the Talbot’s Parking Lot site. 
404 of 445

--- page 405 ---
3 
 
5) What is your expectation of the required ask of the City’s affordable housing fund? 
We would like to work with the City to find the most optimal path for financing to minimize the 
City contribution while still having an expedient path toward construction start. There are some 
unusual nuances in the current financing system, including geographical region allocation limits 
for the 9% tax credit program, which make local gap contributions grow in a non-linear manner 
once over a certain total development cost. If selected, we will model multiple scenarios so the 
City can choose the path with the best value. 
For now, we will use Scenario 2a with 66 homes as a case study on possible financing paths 
based on programs we can access today. 
The ultimate City contribution (after conventional debt, County AHF, and AHP, which all scale 
with increased unit count) will still vary depending on availability of other sources. We maintain 
our AHF assumption of $80k/du, so $5.2 million in this example case: 
 
a) In the most cost-effective scenario from the City’s perspective, MidPen would apply for 
about $4.8 million from a State program (such as TOD), resulting in no additional City 
cash subsidy on top of the ground lease land contribution. This option will lock the 
project on a 4% tax credit path. 
 
b) Presently, tax-exempt bonds are extremely competitive, thus making 4% tax credits 
more difficult to access than 9% tax credits. The City could elect to go the 9% tax credits 
route and contribute $4.4 million to complete the financing stack (at 59% tiebreaker) 
with no state funding and a good likelihood of award. This 9% case is the most 
expedited path toward construction but requires a City contribution. 
 
c) If the County had project-based vouchers (PBV’s) available in the future, we could apply 
for some to reduce or eliminate the City contribution in the 9% case or the State source 
in the 4% case. We will continue to monitor the availability of this and other potential 
sources that could substitute into the stack and present better options for the City. We 
do not anticipate the VASH vouchers we originally contemplated for a senior community 
would be a fit at this family community.  
 
6) What other funding sources will you seek for this project? 
The proposed project is designed to be compatible with both 9% and 4% tax credit financing 
routes to keep options open amidst an uncertain, changing financing environment. Pending 
federal and state legislative changes may make one option more appealing than the other as the 
project goes through pre-development, and we want to position the project to be able to access 
both routes. 
In the 4% financing route, we would seek County of San Mateo AHF, AHP, and one State source 
such as AHSC, TOD, or MHP to complete the financing stack, without any contribution from the 
City. A small City contribution here could mean switching the State source to a less competitive 
one with a smaller award, such as IIG. The 4% financing route requires two non-local 
competitions, for the State source and for tax-exempt bonds. 
405 of 445

--- page 406 ---
4 
 
In the 9% financing route, we would seek only County of San Mateo AHF and AHP, in addition to 
the City contribution to complete the financing stack. This route could be more expeditious, as it 
requires only one non-local competition, for 9% tax credits. 
7) Would you consider trying to acquire the parcels immediately fronting the Talbot’s parking lot along 
B-Street for a larger development site? 
No. The proposed development is feasible as a stand-alone, 100% affordable project with the 
nominal, long-term ground lease from the City. 
8) Would you consider entering into a Project Labor Agreement for this project? 
We would strongly prefer not to. The proposed development will pay State Prevailing Wages 
and employ a majority of union trades, but a PLA will drive up total development cost 
significantly, which will disadvantage the project in state funding competitions where projects 
are ranked based on cost efficiency metrics.  
Paying State Prevailing Wages gives the project the ability to work with a majority of union 
subcontractors, while also creating opportunities for smaller, locally based, and women- or 
minority-owned subcontractors who could be excluded under a PLA. 
MidPen will also prioritize that at least 15% of the design and engineering firms designing the 
development are women- or minority-owned, in alignment with our organization’s value based 
contracting goals.

Surplus Land Notice of Interest – 4th and Railroad Surface Parking Lot 
 
Dear Ms. Kleinbaum: 
 
MidPen Housing would like to express our interest in the 4th and Railroad surface parking lot 
property, APNs 034-179-050 and -060, made available through the Notice of Availability of 
Surplus Land issued January 7, 2021. We believe the site has good affordable housing and public 
benefit potential and would like to collaborate with the City to achieve its goals. 
 
MidPen currently operates Delaware Pacific, Peninsula Station, and St. Matthew Apartments in 
San Mateo (total of 184 affordable homes) and is working with the City now on Kiku Crossing. 
Our partnership with the City and our established presence as part of the community provide us 
great management scale and experience, as well as deep knowledge of the City. 
 
MidPen proposes to partner with Self-Help for the Elderly to relocate their popular senior center 
from the current location in Central Park to occupying the ground floor fronting 4th Avenue at this 
downtown site. Self-Help is an integral part of the San Mateo community, serving seniors in the 
City for many decades. Please see their Letter of Support enclosed. 
 
Above the ground level, we propose to build 100% affordable housing for seniors, a natural 
synergy with Self-Help’s senior center. We have analyzed the site and propose the following 
preliminary program and possible alternate scenarios:

The key assumptions for the recommended program are: 
x 
7 Stories at grade structure (similar height to Kiku Crossing); 
x 
Unit count of roughly 90 homes on 6 residential stories (7 stories total), all restricted for 
seniors, at one-bedroom size; 
x 
100% affordable rental homes restricted to extremely low- and very low-income senior 
households (up to 50% AMI), with at least 20% at extremely low-income (30% AMI); 
x 
We would work with the City and the community on targeting and preferences, and will 
include at least 5% for formerly homeless; 
x 
Long-term ground lease with the City (rather than land donation), so the City will 
maintain permanent control of the land; 
x 
Self-Help for the Elderly will create a senior center of roughly 5,000 square feet on the 
ground floor, and will pay for the hard costs of constructing their space. 
x 
Parking on the ground floor at reduced ratio for the senior population, no replacement 
parking for existing public stalls; 
x 
We intend to use 4% Low Income Housing Tax Credits along with permanent debt, 
available and compatible state programs, County Affordable Housing Fund, and City 
housing funds to finance the development; 
x 
MidPen will pay prevailing wages to all contractors for the construction of the building; 
x 
MidPen will provide in-house property management, to ensure the property operates 
sustainably over the long-term and is accountable to the neighbors; and 
x 
MidPen will also provide in-house resident services, to improve the quality of life of 
residents, coordinate social activities, and assist with aging in place. 
 
The alternate scenarios share the same assumptions, but with two key changes. In the Maximum 
Homes alternate, one-bedroom homes would be replaced with studios. This would increase unit 
yield from 90 to 120 homes, with the trade-off of reducing the private living space for residents 
from roughly 525 to 375 square feet and further reducing the resident parking ratio. In the More 
Parking alternate, the ground level would be maximized for public parking. This would provide 
12 public parking stalls, but at the expense of losing the Self-Help Center space and the 
commercial frontage on 4th Avenue. 
 
To mitigate the reduced residential parking ratio, MidPen would consider implementing measures 
we have experience with at other transit-oriented developments, for example:  
1. Targeted marketing to potential applicants on the limitation of parking; 
2. Inclusion of no-vehicle preference for some of the units; 
3. Provision of transit passes to encourage use of public transit; 
4. Inclusion of space for car sharing (Zipcar or similar); and 
5. Layering of some special needs preferences, such as frail elderly in the County’s Duals 
Program, elderly with physical disabilities, and formerly homeless households.  
 
Thank you for your consideration. 
 
 
 
Jan M. Lindenthal 
Chief Real Estate Development Officer

Self-Help for the Elderly expresses support for MidPen Housing Corporation’s Letter of

relocate our Senior Center from Central Park to the 4th and Railroad surplus site, occupying
approximately 5000 sq. Ft on the ground floor, fronting 4th Ave.
Self-Help has operated the Central Park Senior Center since 1992, almost 30 years. We
serve 1300-1500 seniors in San Mateo each year with a comprehensive range of social
activities, educational classes, congregate and home-delivered meals, social services and
wellness programs to primarily Asian, low to moderate income seniors and retirees who live
in San Mateo and nearby cities. We also started a senior lunch program with daily activities
in Millbrae four years ago. Since 1992, the San Mateo Health Insurance Counseling and
Assistance Programs (HICAP) has helped thousands of seniors each year with the Medicare
enrollment and other Medicare related issues.
Our San Mateo/Central Park Senior Center’s current lease with San Mateo Recreation and
Park Department will expire at the end of 2025, and we have been actively searching for a
permanent home for the center for a long time. When we were contacted by Felix at MidPen
back in January with the possibility of a partnership to relocate our current center to the new
development at 4th and Railroad, we were overjoyed, and our Board of Directors discussed
the offer and approved staff to move forward with MidPen’s proposal.

Surplus Land Act Follow-Up Proposal 
Page 1 of 3 
Mercy Housing California is pleased to present more details about our proposed project concept for the 4th and 
Railroad site in San Mateo. To better understand the site, Van Meter Williams Pollack LLP (VMWP) completed a 
site study, which we have included. VMWP is very experienced in designing affordable housing and mixed-use 
developments and has designed many MHC buildings throughout the Bay Area. The site study that VMWP 
prepared offers an efficient plan while providing for the residential and nonprofit spaces. The ground floor of 
the building would provide a residential lobby and management offices, the Self-Help for the Elderly space, and 
parking. The second floor would include residential community space; laundry, community room, and courtyard, 
and residential units. Floors three through seven would be entirely residential.   
The size of the site is small but could support an efficient building that can generally meet the parameters the 
City set forth. The VMWP study shows how the site could accommodate multiple ground floor uses; lobby, 
offices, and nonprofit space, and parking. However, it would be difficult to maintain access to the rear parking 
area for the businesses fronting B Street, while accommodating the desired ground-floor uses and unit count. 
We are working with a broker to explore purchasing any of the neighboring parcels. Acquiring an additional 
parcel would provide more flexibility to meet the City’s needs, and would allow for a site design that provides 
access to existing businesses.   
1)
How many units do you propose to build?
Based on the requested parameters, VMWP estimated 69 units in a 7-story building with a range of unit
sizes, from studios to 3-bedrooms. The unit mix that VMWP proposed is below. It includes 25% 3-
bedrooms, which is a requirement for many state funding sources. Since the site is relatively small, there
are more studio and 1-bedroom units than we may typically include in a family property. A 69-unit
development offers the flexibility to be funded through a 4% or 9% credit pathway. This development
size is also operationally efficient, allowing it to be self-supporting and sustainable.

2)
What income ranges would your project accommodate?
MHC anticipates that range of incomes served at this site would range from 30%-80% of AMI. Based on
the market study that we have from our nearby Middlefield Junction development, we anticipate that
the 80% AMI rents may be close to market. Before confirming the income range, we would work with
the City and County to identify the desired income range and do a market study for this site to confirm
the rent range. Additionally, we would explore whether the City or County want to set aside the 30%
AMI units for a special needs population, supported by vouchers or an operating subsidy.

Surplus Land Act Follow-Up Proposal 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Page 2 of 3 
 
 
3) How much on-site parking are you able to provide for the residential units? 
Based on the site yield from VMWP, the site could provide up to 33 spaces of parking. This plan uses 26 
stackers, allowing for the larger units to have space for two cars. Without including the extra space with 
the stackers, this is a 0.5:1 parking ratio. With this amount of parking, the ground-floor could also 
accommodate up to 3,000 square feet of nonprofit space. If the nonprofit space increases, the amount 
of parking would have to decrease. The site study shows an alternate of 4,300 square feet of nonprofit 
space and 27 parking spaces.  
MHC understands the importance of providing adequate parking at the site as well as the nonprofit 
space for Self-Help for the Elderly. We will work with all the stakeholders to find the best balance of 
uses.  
4) Describe any on-site amenities associated with your project. 
Mercy Housing provides resident services and amenities at all of our buildings. This includes a 
welcoming lobby, offices for our on-site property management and resident services staff, and 
community space. At the 4th and Railroad site, amenities would include a large resident courtyard on the 
second floor with connections to a community room and laundry room. The ground floor will provide a 
resident lobby with offices for on-site property management and resident service staff. The Mercy 
Housing staff will provide programming and support for the residents, activating the second-floor 
community space. Additionally, we would explore intergenerational programming with the Self-Help for 
the Elderly. We have several sites throughout the Bay Area with intergenerational programming, and 
offers benefits for the seniors and youth that participate.  
5) What is your expectation of the required ask of the City’s affordable housing fund? 
MHC’s expectation of the required ask from the City’s affordable housing fund is $3,000,000 or 
approximately $43,000 per unit. We believe that this is a typical level of support for affordable housing 
in the City and would allow us to leverage other financing sources.  
6) What other funding sources will you seek for this project? 
In addition to support from the City’s affordable housing fund, we would seek funding from the County 
of San Mateo. The County of San Mateo has funded our Middlefield Junction family development and 
has expressed support for this project. We would also pursue funding from the State Department of 
Housing and Community Development (HCD). This site would be very competitive for Infill Infrastructure 
Grant funding. MHC has been awarded IIG funding for seven developments. We would also explore 
using MHP or AHSC funds depending on the ultimate size of the project and funding gap. The County has 
especially expressed support for a 9% tax credit project and we would explore both a 4% and 9% tax 
credit option.  
7) Would you consider trying to acquire the parcels immediately fronting the Talbot’s parking lot along B-
Street for a larger development site? 
416 of 445

--- page 417 ---
Surplus Land Act Follow-Up Proposal 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Page 3 of 3 
 
 
We are already speaking with a broker to exploring acquiring adjacent parcels to the site to provide 
more flexibility to the development. As discussed in our introduction, this site is relatively small, 
particularly if parking access needs to be provided for the businesses on B Street. Acquiring an adjacent 
parcel would be ideal for the project’s success. 
8) Would you consider entering into a Project Labor Agreement for this project? 
Mercy Housing has worked with Project Labor Agreements on our projects in the past. We know that 
the terms of a PLA can vary and we would be willing to work with the City on an agreement that best 
serves the development.

Mercy Housing California is pleased to submit this Notice of Interest to the City of San Mateo for the two 
surplus land parcels located at 4th and Railroad (“Talbot’s Lot”) totaling approximately 16,600 square 
feet in size. 
Mercy Housing History and Background 
Founded by the Sisters of Mercy in 1981, Mercy Housing, Inc. (MHI) is the nation’s largest nonprofit 
affordable housing developer and owner.  From its statewide headquarters in San Francisco, Mercy 
Housing develops, manages, and provides resident services throughout California.    
 
Mercy Housing’s mission is to create stable, vibrant and healthy communities by developing, financing 
and operating affordable, program-enriched housing for families, seniors and people with special needs 
who lack the economic resources to access quality, safe housing opportunities.  MHI builds and acquires 
affordable housing through five Regional Development Corporations based in California, Georgia, Illinois 
and Washington.  MHI owns and provides property management to more than 23,000 units of 
affordable housing and currently serves more than 50,000 people on any given day.   
 
Mercy Housing California (“MHC”) was incorporated in 1988 as the California affiliate of MHI.   MHC has 
developed over 11,000 homes and has 6,700 units in development.  MHC brings to the table a unique 
combination of national scope and local presence-- with offices in the Bay Area, Los Angeles, and 
Sacramento.   Because of our strong track record and financial strength, MHC consistently succeeds in 
securing both competitive public funding and the most advantageous terms from private lenders and 
investors.  In terms of financial strength, MHC is backed by the full strength and credit of MHI.  As of 
December 31, 2019, MHI had, on a consolidated basis, total assets in excess of $232 billion, cash and 
cash equivalents of $96 million and unrestricted net assets-controlling of $336 million. 
 
Real estate experience and capacity 
In the Bay Area, MHC has developed nearly 6,000 units in over 80 communities, including six completed 
developments in San Mateo County.  In San Mateo County, MHC’s current development activity includes 
the redevelopment of a 50-acre campus in Burlingame, 93 units of new educator workforce housing in 
Palo Alto, and 179 units of family housing in unincorporated Redwood City. 
422 of 445

--- page 423 ---
In terms of capacity, MHC has a local team of 35 real estate staff to support our development pipeline, 
including dedicated staff for community engagement at the entitlement stage of development.  MHC 
also has the unique ability to draw on a large national team that includes legal, accounting, and finance.   
Through Mercy Housing Management Group, Mercy Housing brings the experience and perspective of a 
long-term owner to the development process.      
 
Resident Services 
As much as these elements are critical to our success, it is our commitment to our residents that truly 
differentiates Mercy Housing from other affordable housing developers.  With 25 years of experience in 
providing resident programs, Mercy Housing has learned that the most successful programs speak 
directly to residents' concerns, interests and needs.  
 
Mercy Housing’s Resident Services Program serves over 4,400 seniors and is implemented at 43 senior 
properties throughout California.  Mercy Housing is consistently sought out as a partner by senior 
organizations and health care providers because our resident programming effectively supports both 
healthy and frail seniors through a seamless blend of social and health-related services. 
 
Community Outreach 
Mercy Housing California and our design team have extensive experience working with and through 
community outreach plans.  Virtually every one of MHC’s developments, including our own property 
rehabs, require thoughtful engagement with stakeholders, be they residents, neighbors, community 
organizations or businesses. Staff invests time and resources in community education about affordable 
housing, creating long term community relationships, and involving community members in creating 
housing that meet the needs of the community and the future residents.   
 
Proposed Project Details 
The location and size of the Talbot’s Lot are ideal for senior affordable housing project. The site is 
centrally located to allow seniors access to amenities and services. The smaller size of the site is well 
suited to the smaller units in senior housing developments. MHC anticipates that we could develop at 
least a 60-unit senior affordable housing project on the site. Based on our discussions with the County of 
San Mateo, there is support for senior affordable housing here. Depending on financing type this senior 
affordable housing would have a portion of the units set-aside to serve seniors experiencing 
homelessness. The affordability of the units would range from 30% AMI to 60% AMI, with an average 
affordability of approximately 45% AMI.   
 
MHC has worked closely with the County of San Mateo, which has funded our existing properties in the 
County of San Mateo. We also explored applying for HUD 202 financing for this site, which would 
provide capital and subsidy funds for the project. This project will be financed through Low Income 
Housing Tax Credits. The County of San Mateo would support its submission as a 9% or 4% and MHC 
would explore which pathway would be the best option.  
 
423 of 445

--- page 424 ---
We would expect that a 60-unit project would be the minimum size of the project here but would 
happily work with the City of San Mateo to identify increasing the size of the project. This site is eligible 
for SB 35 streamlining, which would allow for a faster entitlement process and allow the potential 
development to be larger than 60-units, if the City supported that.  
Purchase Price 
Based on a recent appraisal of a site that MHC is developing in Burlingame, MHC is offering a purchase 
price for the two parcels, totaling 16,600 sf of $4,200,000.  
Alternatively, MHC would be interested in executing a long-term ground lease with the City of San 
Mateo with annual ground lease payments, if the City would be interested in maintaining ownership of 
the land.

Re: Talbots City Lot — Letter of Support for Harvest Properties
Dear City Council Members,

I am writing on behalf of Self-Help for the Elderly, a non-profit organization that has been providing
assistance and support to seniors in the San Francisco Bay Area since 1966. The purpose of this letter is
to let the City Council Members know of our strong support for Harvest Properties proposed project at the
Talbots City Lot in Downtown San Mateo.

I recently had a series of conversations with Harvest Properties, who is proposing to build ~52 affordable
housing units with ground floor community serving retail on the City Parking Lot next to the Talbots site.
This is a very exciting project and meets the needs and objectives of the City and community of San
Mateo, as it will provide much needed affordable housing, activate the public realm and retail experience,
and transform the City block to energize the Downtown neighborhood.

Additionally, I believe the ground floor community space is an ideal choice for Self-Help’s new Senior
and Recreational Center and office. As many of you may know, the building we currently occupy in San
Mateo Central Park will be demolished in a few years, so we have been in search of a new space for our
seniors. I have provided the Harvest team with an overview of our space and program requirements. The
Harvest team was collaborative, responsive, and sensitive to our needs, and is currently working with
their architect to design the ground-floor to accommodate our uses. I am confident in their ability to
deliver a high-quality space that we can call home for years to come.

The adjacent location to Central Park will also provide our participants with easy access to outdoor fitness
and dance activities. Additionally the space would be offered to us at a rent significantly below market
rate, which provides substantial financial contribution to our organization, and will allow us to deploy
more reserves on the space build-out, new furniture procurement and operations to better serve our
community.

I hope you will join us in supporting this project because it is the kind of development that enhances the
Downtown neighborhood and will support Self-Help for the Elderly to continue to serve San Mateo’s
senior community to live longer, healthier, and more purposeful lives. Thank you very much.

Senate and Assembly bills brought forward by the Legislative Committee.
BACKGROUND:
The Legislative Committee met on May 24th to discuss pending bills and determine which of those bills should be brought 
forward for consideration by the entire City Council.  Overall, 15 bills were selected to be brought forward and focus on 
the following policy areas:


bill, which was originally a housing-related bill, has been amended.  The amended bill is now related to planning for the

applicable to SB 30, 31, and 32, introduced by Senator Cortese; SB 68 and 596, introduced by Senator Becker; and AB 550 
and 629, introduced by Assemblymember Chiu.

There is no direct budgetary impact from providing letters of support or opposition to any of these bills.

Senate Bill 8 (Senator Nancy Skinner) 
 
Summary of Legislation:  
Senate Bill 8 (SB8) is a follow-up to Senate Bill 330 (SB 330), which Senator Skinner sponsored in 2019 
and went into effect in January 2020. SB 330 was aimed at streamlining housing production and made 
changes to how local jurisdictions can review residential applications and limited a city or county’s 
ability to change land use designations in a way that would reduce the intensity of residential uses 
beyond the codes that were in place as of January 1, 2018. 
 
SB 8 extends the sunset of the provisions of SB 330 until 2030 (the Act was previously set to expire in 
2025) and clarifies some of the language included in the original bill. Principally, this includes more 
clearly defining a “housing development project,” to also include single dwelling units and both 
ministerial and discretionary projects. 
 
Impact to San Mateo:  
There are currently two SB330 planning applications in the development pipeline in San Mateo: 
• 
Peninsula Heights (PA20-012) – Planning application approved for 290 multifamily units  
• 
1919 O’Farrell (PA20-037) – Planning application under review for 49 multifamily units 
 
Under SB 330, the City is limited to a maximum of five hearings in connection to their approval, and the 
planning applications are subject to the general plan, zoning standards, and fee schedule in place at the 
time of the submittal of their pre-application. If SB 8 is signed into law, developers would have until 
January 1, 2030 to submit pre-applications for future projects, and the City would be required to follow 
a similar process in reviewing those planning applications. 
 
League of California Cities Position:  
SB 8 is on the League of California Cities’ watch list, but the League has taken no position at this time. 
 
 
429 of 445

--- page 430 ---
Senate Bill 9 (Senators Toni Atkins, Anna Caballero, Susan Rubio, Scott Wiener) 
 
Summary of Legislation:  
Senate Bill 9 (SB 9) is aimed at accelerating the production of duplexes and ADUs under certain 
conditions to help alleviate California’s housing constraints. In practical terms, SB 9 requires two things:  
1. First, it requires ministerial approval of duplexes on parcels zoned for single-family residential 
use under certain conditions. Local agencies can still require those new homes to conform to 
existing objective standards, but those standards cannot preclude the development from 
including up to two units of at least 800 square feet each or require setbacks that would 
physically preclude the development from including up to two units. 
2. Second, local agencies would be required to ministerially approve a parcel subdivision (“urban 
lot split”) on parcels that are zoned for single family residential uses so long as neither of the 
resulting parcels is smaller than 1,200 square feet (among other criteria). 
 
The stated goal of this legislation is to streamline the process whereby a homeowner could create a 
duplex or subdivide their lot to create neighborhood housing. Local jurisdictions would be permitted to 
impose an owner-occupancy requirement for up to one year after the approval of an urban lot split, and 
any rental unit created under this bill would be required to be rented for a term of at least 30 days. 
 
Impact to San Mateo: 
Should SB 9 become law, the owners of single family parcels in San Mateo would have the ability to 
apply for a parcel subdivision and/or construct duplexes. The City would be required to ministerially 
approve the parcel subdivisions, construction of duplexes, or conversion of existing single family homes 
to duplexes that meet the requirements of SB 9. Such duplexes could be subjected to objective design 
standards.  
 
League of California Cities Position:  
The League of California Cities has submitted a letter of opposition. Primarily, the League opposes the 
removal of local discretion in approving certain types of residential developments and disagrees with SB 
9’s provision that would require ministerial approval of an urban lot split.  
 
 
430 of 445

--- page 431 ---
Senate Bill 10 (Senator Scott Wiener) 
 
Summary of Legislation:  
Senate Bill 10 (SB 10) allows local jurisdictions to pass an ordinance, resolution, or local regulation to 
upzone certain parcels for up to ten units of residential density. Such a change would not be subject to 
the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA) provided the parcels are either: 
• 
located within a half mile of a major transit site or high-quality bus corridor, or 
• 
an urban infill site. 
 
Contrary to past proposals, SB 10 does not compel cities or counties to make these changes to their 
zoning codes, but it does lay out the process by which the changes could be made. A local jurisdiction 
would be required to specify the allowable building height on the affected parcels and, “make a finding 
that the increased density authorized by the ordinance is consistent with the city or county’s obligation 
to affirmatively further fair housing.” Should a local government wish to pass such an ordinance, it 
would need to do so prior to January 1, 2029. 
 
Lastly, as currently written, SB 10 would allow local jurisdictions to make these zoning changes even if 
there are voter-approved zoning restrictions already in place that might otherwise prevent such 
upzoning.  
 
Impact to San Mateo: 
As SB 10 does not compel cities to change zoning codes or upzone for residential uses, there would only 
be impacts should the City Council decide to exercise the authority granted by SB 10.  
 
League of California Cities Position:  
SB 10 is on the league of California Cities’ watch list, but the League has taken no position at this time. 
 
 
 
 
431 of 445

--- page 432 ---
Assembly Bill 1401 (Assembly Member Laura Friedman) 
 
Summary of Legislation:  
Assembly Bill 1401 (AB 1401) is intended to reduce a significant construction cost; namely, the provision 
of on-site parking. Hundreds of local jurisdictions in California impose minimum parking requirements 
on developers to ensure that the proposed construction will be able to accommodate the parking 
demands of future residents and/or patrons. AB 1401 would prohibit local jurisdictions from imposing 
minimum parking requirements on parcels that are within a half mile walking distance of a major transit 
stop or high-quality transit corridor.  
 
Supporters of AB 1401, including its sponsor, Assembly Member Laura Friedman, cite studies that point 
to an existing abundance of parking across the State and the oversupply of parking at several recently-
constructed commercial and residential projects. Given current construction costs, advocates view the 
reduction of parking production as a way to lower the cost of building housing while also reducing 
congestion and pollution by encouraging more people to utilize public transportation. 
 
As written, the bill does not preclude a developer from voluntarily providing parking in these transit-rich 
areas, nor does it eliminate a local jurisdiction’s ability to set requirements for how much of that parking 
should be set aside for electric vehicles or car shares. 
 
Impact to San Mateo: 
Under SMMC 27.64, the City of San Mateo imposes off-street parking requirements for a variety of land 
uses, with requirements differing for those within the Central Parking Improvement District (CPID). 
Alternatively, those projects in the CPID are permitted to pay a fee in-lieu of the required on-site 
parking. 
 
However, because AB 1401 would prohibit cities from imposing minimum parking requirements on 
those parcels within a half mile of major transit stops or high-quality transit corridors, the City would no 
longer be able to impose such requirements in the CPID (and other areas of the City). All three of the 
City’s Caltrain stations would qualify as major transit stops, as defined in AB 1401, and El Camino Real is 
a high-quality transit corridor by virtue of the frequent bus service provided by SamTrans. As a result, 
significant portions of the City would no longer be subject to minimum parking requirements. 
 
Although many developers would likely provide parking voluntarily, the lack of minimum requirements 
could have ripple effects that would impact parking for the surrounding areas. 
 
League of California Cities Position:  
The League of California Cities has submitted a letter of opposition. The League cites the following three 
primary reasons for its opposition: 
1. Proximity to public transit does not necessarily guarantee access to that transit 
2. Transit agencies would have significant impact on a local jurisdiction’s parking requirements and 
could alter the geographic scope of those requirements by changing transit routes 
3. Local agencies are more closely connected to community needs and better equipped to set 
parking requirements at an appropriate level

SB 30 – Building Decarbonization (Senator Dave Cortese of District 15) 
 
Summary of Legislation: 
 
This bill would prohibit State agencies from supporting, funding, designing, or constructing projects that 
are connected to the natural gas grid. It will also require the California State Building Decarbonization 
Plan to be developed and put into effect by January 1, 2035. On or after January 1, 2023, to the extent 
permissible under federal law, a state agency shall not provide funding or other support for projects for 
the construction of residential or nonresidential buildings that are connected to the natural gas grid. 
 
Impact to San Mateo: 
 
This may impact City projects, such as affordable housing developments or City facilities, that receive a 
portion of their funding from the State. We would assume that all buildings are on the natural gas grid 
except for residential and office buildings which are to be all electric. This bill would affect reach codes 
of any new buildings.  
 
League of California Cities Position: 
 
California League of Cities has not yet published an opinion on this legislation.

SB 31 - Building Decarbonization (Senator Dave Cortese of District 15) 
 
Summary of Legislation: 
 
This bill would extend clean energy and energy efficiency funds that are available through the federal 
government due to addressing the economic recovery and development of the COVID-19 pandemic, as 
well as award funds, funded by electric utility ratepayers, through commissions to the development and 
deployment of new and old commercial and residential building decarbonization.  
 
This bill would:  
• 
Require the Energy Commission to create and implement programs to promote existing and 
new building decarbonization. 
• 
Authorize the Energy Commission, depending on if the clean energy or energy efficiency funds 
are made available from the federal government to address economic recovery and 
development due to the COVID-19 pandemic, to expend federal money for projects for existing 
and new building decarbonization. 
• 
Require the Energy Commission, under the EPIC program, to award funds for projects that lead 
to the development and deployment of commercial and residential building decarbonization 
technologies and investments that reduce or eliminate greenhouse gas generation in those 
buildings. 
• 
Authorize the expenditure of revenues as required of electrical corporations by the CPUC and 
nonbypassable usage-based charges on distribution services by local publicly owned electric 
utilities for existing and new building decarbonization. 
Impact to San Mateo: 
 
This bill may result in available grant funding for building decarbonization programs and projects that 
the City may be able to take advantage of. This bill will help provide funds to create cleaner energy in 
buildings, both old and new. The buildings that the funds would be used on is to be determined.  
League of California Cities Position: 
California League of Cities has not yet published an opinion on this legislation.

SB 32 – Energy General Plan: Building Decarbonization Requirements (Senator Dave Cortese of District 
15) 
 
Summary of Legislation:  
 
This bill will:  
• 
Commencing January 1, 2023, this bill would require a city or county to make a one-time 
amendment to the appropriate elements of its general plan, climate action or greenhouse gas 
emissions reduction plan, or building or other codes to include goals, policies, objectives, 
targets, and feasible implementation strategies, as specified, to decarbonize newly constructed 
commercial and residential buildings. 
• 
Require a city or county to submit these draft amendments to the State Energy Resources 
Conservation and Development Commission at least 45 days before the adoption of them. 
• 
Require the legislative body of a city or county to listen to the Commission’s advisory comments 
and to take them into consideration before adopting them. 
 
The proposed building decarbonization amendments must include the following: 
• 
A report describing greenhouse gas emissions data for existing commercial and residential 
buildings, including significant sources of the emissions. 
• 
A summary of local, state, and federal policies, programs, and regulations that may assist in the 
decarbonization of existing and newly constructed commercial and residential buildings. 
• 
A comprehensive set of goals, policies, and objectives that may assist in decarbonizing newly 
constructed commercial and residential buildings.   
• 
A set of feasible implementation measures designed to carry out those goals, policies, and 
objectives. 
Impact to San Mateo:  
 
The City of San Mateo has already adopted building decarbonization goals, policies, and strategies in our 
Climate Action Plan which is an Appendix to our General Plan. It is unclear if the City would need to 
submit these previously adopted documents to the State Commission for review and comment.

Bill Number: 
 
AB 43 - Traffic Safety (Assembly member Laura Friedman of the 43rd District) 
 
Summary of Legislation: 
 
This bill amends  eight sections and adds  four sections to the California Vehicle Code to enhance traffic 
safety, particularly in the realm of speed limit setting policy.  
 
California’s current policy for establishing speed limits on State highways or local roadways follows a 
prescriptive process that can generally be broken into two types – 1) statutory speed limits and 2) speed 
limits set by an “engineering and traffic survey” (E&TS).  
 
For roadways where the State or local authorities wish to establish a speed limit that differs from the 
statutory speed limit, an E&TS is required to justify the proposed speed limit. 
 
Proposed Legislation 
The changes proposed under AB 43 generally apply to the second type of speed limit described above – 
speed limits set by an engineering and traffic survey. These changes include: 
 
• Expanding the types of required information included in an E&TS to include consideration for 
residential density, if applicable, bicycles and pedestrians, particularly vulnerable pedestrian 
groups such as children, seniors, persons with disabilities, users of personal assistive mobility 
devices, and the unhoused. 
• Loosening the requirement to align proposed speed limits with prevailing speeds where one of 
the following conditions apply: 
o High concentrations of injuries have occurred, or 
o Adjacent land uses generate high concentrations of bicyclists and pedestrians, especially 
those from vulnerable groups such as children, seniors, persons with disabilities, and the 
unhoused, or  
o It is determined that previously established speeds may continue to suffice for that 
roadway segment 
o Providing additional options for proposed speed limits, including 15 and 20 mile per hour 
speed limits 
• Providing additional flexibility in the criteria that must be met to propose a speed limit below the 
statutory 25 mile per hour speed limit in the vicinity of schools. The bill also expands this 
allowance to establish speeds lower than the statutory speed limit beyond just school zones to 
include “business activity zones.” 
 
Impact to San Mateo: 
To reduce traffic congestion and promote alternative modes of transportation, the City of San Mateo has 
prioritized bicycle and pedestrian safety and improvements in the public right-of-way. While adoption of 
bicycle and pedestrian plans identify needs to enhance and close gaps in each of these networks, the 
relationship between these and the vehicle networks continue to present a challenge, particularly as it 
relates to vehicle speeds. The State’s current, stringent practice requires speed limits be set at speeds 
comfortable for motorists and generally without the context of other roadway users and thereby creates an 
inequity for those other roadway users. Assembly Bill 43 takes significant strides toward addressing this 
inequity. 
439 of 445

--- page 440 ---
While the City’s current speed limit setting practice aligns with State law, staff have recently evaluated the 
need for a City-specific speed limit policy (Policy) to clarify items within State law. The Policy would generally 
identify paths to maintain lowered speed limits in an effort to align with the City’s goals of prioritizing bicycle 
and pedestrian safety. The changes proposed in AB 43 align closely with the goals of the forthcoming Policy 
to be presented to City Council. If passed, AB 43 would likely require changes to the proposed Policy if 
adopted. However, it is not expected that the changes would be contrary to current efforts, rather expand 
on them and provide additional flexibility when considering speed limit changes.  
 
League of California Cities Position: 
The League of California Cities has not taken a position on this bill. 
 
 
440 of 445

--- page 441 ---
Bill Number: 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
May be acted upon in Jan 2022 
 
AB 550 – Speed Safety System Pilot Program (Assemblymember David Chiu of San Francisco) 
 
Summary 
 
This bill authorizes Los Angeles, Oakland, San Jose, San Francisco, and one yet-to-be determined city in 
southern California to establish a Speed Safety Pilot Program for speed-limit enforcement in certain areas 
until January 1, 2027. The goal of this legislation is to reduce excessive speed and related serious 
injury/fatality collisions in a more equitable manner. 
 
Presently, traffic speed enforcement, which is critical to reduce speed violations, is conducted by police 
officers; however, research has confirmed a well-documented disparate impact of this enforcement on 
communities of color. There are tools, such as speed safety systems (primarily cameras, radar, and laser 
technology), that can assist cities in addressing excessive speeding and speed related crashes in a more 
equitable manner. These speed safety systems, when utilized in conjunction with education and traffic 
engineering, have been proven to significantly reduce speeding, improve traffic safety, and prevent traffic 
related fatalities and injuries. 
 
Impact to San Mateo: 
 
There would be no initial impact for the City of San Mateo. The impact would come after 2027, if the City 
decides to implement the program. Based on the bill’s verbiage, the responsibility for oversight of this 
program during the pilot phase rests with the involved cities’ Public Works Departments as the ‘local 
department of transportation.’ If this program is extended state-wide, it would remain to be determined 
which department in San Mateo would have responsibility for program oversight. 
 
Prior to implementation, the City would be required to do several things, including: 
• 
Adopt a Speed Safety Use Policy and a Speed Safety System Impact Report 
o Work with relevant stakeholders in the development of this report 
• 
Assess equity impacts on local neighborhoods from program operation 
• 
Engage in a Public Information Campaign for at least 30 days 
• 
Issue warning notices for 30 days 
• 
Develop uniform guidelines for the processing and storage of confidential information. 
 
Financial/Legal/Operational Impact 
 
Citations issued under this program would be civil in nature and would not result in ‘points’ on driver’s 
records, or license suspensions/revocations. The bill specifies certain citation amounts, based on the speed 
involved in the violation. This could result in reduced revenue from speed citation issuance, which would 
need further study to confirm. Depending on the scale of San Mateo’s adoption of such a program, there 
would be a need to add staffing to address program administration and maintenance. 
 
For context, data on speed related collisions and citations are listed below.

Bill Number:   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
May be acted upon in Jan 2022 
 
AB 629 - San Francisco Bay Area: Public Transportation (Assemblymember David Chiu of San Francisco) 
 
Summary of Legislation: 
 
California Assembly Bill 629 (AB 629) is known as the Seamless and Resilient Bay Area Transit Act. The bill 
aims to create a more seamless and resilient public transportation network through improvement in the 
coordination between the 27 transit agencies throughout the nine Bay Area counties. Under the bill, the 
Metropolitan Transportation Commission (MTC) would be directed to work with the transportation agencies 
in the region to build a connected and coordinated regional public transportation system.  
 
The current draft of AB 629 outlines the following actions: 
1. MTC would be required to develop a transit priority network for the region that designates corridors 
to provide faster and more reliable transit, including ensuring regional managed lanes support the 
region’s transit system by January 1, 2024. 
2. MTC would work to align transit fare structure and costs, by submitting the Fare Coordination and 
Integration Study to the Senate Transportation Committee by February 2022, providing progress to 
date on implementation by January 2023, and creating a pilot accumulator pass between operators 
by July 2023.  
3. MTC would be required to work with transit agencies to develop a comprehensive standardized 
regional transit mapping and wayfinding system, including branding, design standards, and an 
implementation and maintenance strategy on or before July 1, 2024. By July 1, 2025, transit agencies 
shall convert to using only this system unless MTC adopts an alternate deployment timeline.  
4. Transit operators would be required to make real-time transit data available and report to MTC by 
January 1, 2023 on the ability to predict transit schedules using real-time data. 
 
 
Impact to San Mateo: 
 
With numerous transit agencies operating in and through the City of San Mateo and San Mateo County, 
connectivity and seamless mobility between services would be an integral step in increasing transit 
ridership. Establishing a shared fare system, coordinated wayfinding, and incentives for frequent transit use 
complements the commute patterns typically seen in the Bay Area, and streamlines a complex system to 
make travel more intuitive and smoother for San Mateo users.  
 
MTC has made efforts in the past to increase coordination and connectivity between Bay Area transit 
agencies, but separate mapping, scheduling, fare structures, and costs continue to make navigating transfers 
between services confusing for users. While the contents of the bill may be modified, the structure of 
requiring incremental steps over the next few years may support the progress toward a coordinated regional 
system that mirrors how transit trips actually take place.  
 
While AB 629 contains critical components to develop a safe and seamless transit system, the bill is not yet 
complete as the recommendations from the Task Force have not yet been published, and the bill is not set 
to be considered again by the Appropriations Committee until January 2022. The bill may be modified when 
443 of 445

--- page 444 ---
the Task Force recommendations are published so the bill is currently on hold. Council may want to consider 
not taking a position on this bill at this time.  
 
League of California Cities Position: 
 
The League of California Cities has not taken a position on this bill. SPUR, Transform, the Bay Area Council, and 
the Silicon Valley Leadership Group have issued letters of support for the bill.

Bill Number:  
 
AB 1238 – Pedestrian Access (Assemblymember Phil Ting of San Francisco) 
 
Summary of Legislation: 
 
This bill would remove certain prohibitions on jaywalking and remove the authority of local jurisdictions to 
adopt related ordinances.  
 
Existing law prohibits the following for pedestrians: 
 
• 
Entering the roadway if the pedestrian is facing a steady circular yellow or yellow arrow warning 
signal unless otherwise directed by a pedestrian control signal, as specified.  
• 
Crossing at any place except a crosswalk between adjacent intersections controlled by traffic 
control signal devices or by police officers. 
• 
Suddenly leaving a curb or other place of safety and walking or running into the path of a vehicle 
that is so close as to constitute an immediate hazard. 
 
This bill would change the vehicle code to only require that pedestrians do the following: 
 
• 
Yield the right-of-way to all vehicles upon the roadway that are so near as to constitute an 
immediate hazard. 
o Immediate hazard would be defined as existing “if the approaching vehicle is so near or 
is approaching so fast that a reasonably careful person would realize that there is a 
danger of collision.” 
 
Impact to San Mateo: 
 
AB 1238 would change the way PD staff enforces jaywalking related laws, requiring a consideration of 
whether alleged violations meet the ‘immediate hazard’ threshold. It would also likely require revision 
of section 11.52 of the Municipal Code. 
 
Based on a review of collision and citation data from the past few years, there is not a significant impact 
based on jaywalking enforcement.
